Refutation of Dualists: The Inconsistency
Maturidi exposes a fatal flaw in opponents' anti-dualism argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi exposes the inconsistency in his opponents' argument against dualism, showing it actually leads to dualism.

Core Idea
Opponents of dualism inadvertently prove dualism true.

Maturidi opens by recalling how earlier scholars argued against the dualists, but then he points out a fatal flaw in their reasoning. Their attempt to derive good and evil from one source ends up conceding the very dualist position they sought to refute. This lesson examines that first step of his critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the first few lines of the source up to 'فقد أوجب ما قالت الثنوية'. Notice how Maturidi narrates his opponents' argument and then turns it back on them.
Focus on the logical flow: they start with a premise and end with a conclusion they did not intend.
2
Identify the opponents' original argument
The opponents argued that both good and evil can come from one source (God) to reject dualism. Find that statement in the text.
The phrase 'بجواز كون الخير والشر والطيب والخبيث من واحد'.
3
Notice the turn of reasoning
Maturidi says 'ثم رجعوا إلى إحالة أحد الوجهين عن الله' — they ended up ascribing one aspect away from God. Why does this contradict their earlier stance?
This shows they did not consistently maintain that one source accounts for both.
4
Understand the final consequence
Maturidi concludes: 'فمن حقق الوجه الآخر في الموجود في العالم فقد أوجب ما قالت الثنوية.' Whoever affirms the other aspect (evil) in the world has made dualism necessary.
So their own reasoning leads to dualism, not away from it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لم يزل أئمتهم كلموا الثنوية بجواز كون الخير والشر والطيب والخبيث من واحد ليدفعوا به القول بالاثنين، ثم رجعوا إلى إحالة أحد الوجهين عن الله. فمن حقق الوجه الآخر في الموجود في العالم فقد أوجب ما قالت الثنوية. ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم دفع ما عورض بخلق شيء لا ينتفع به بما لم يدل ذلك على تكليف الزمن. فهذا يبين أن الذي قال ببديهة العقل كذب، وأنه إنما ادعى القياس على ما وافقه خصمه عليه لا غير. ثم حصل بما عارض به خصمه ـ مما ادعى ببديهة العقل - على ما يثبت شيئا صدقاً، مما هو كذب في الشاهد. ثم أجاب بما يوجد احتراز النفع بالفعل بأنه لا يحمد عليه. ثبت أن ما قبح منه لم يقبح لعينه. وكذلك يجد خصمه مما يقدر عليه ولا يجوز التكليف به، ثبت أن ذلك لم يقبح لنفسه، نحو الفواحش والكفر. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وذا أولى، لما يجئ مثل هذا من الصغار ولا يلحقه وصف فُحش ولا كفر، ولا يجئ منهم فعل غير نافع لنفسه يوصف بالحكمة والذي قال من احتراز النفع فهو من ذلك الوجه حكمة، ولكن من وجه الضرر.
Then their leaders continued to speak to the dualists about the permissibility of good and evil, pleasant and foul coming from one source, in order to refute the belief in two principles. Then they turned to ascribing one of the two aspects away from God. So whoever verified the other aspect in what exists in the world has made necessary what the dualists said. And there is no power except by Allah. Then he repelled the objection regarding the creation of something not beneficial by saying that does not indicate the burdening of the disabled. This shows that the one who spoke by the intuition of reason lied, and that he only claimed analogy on what his opponent agreed with him upon, nothing else. Then by what he opposed his opponent with, claiming it by intuition of reason, he established something true, while it is false in the witnessed. Then he answered by saying that avoiding benefit in an action is not praiseworthy. It is established that what is ugly from him is not ugly in itself. Likewise, he finds his opponent able to commit abominations and disbelief, but burdening him with them is not permissible. So it is established that those things are not ugly in themselves. And there is no power except by Allah. And this is more appropriate, because such things come from smallness and are not described as foul or disbelief, and no action that is of no benefit to oneself is described as wisdom. So what is said about avoiding benefit is wisdom in that respect, but from the aspect of harm.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's final refutation of his opponents' arguments. He recounts how the opponents (probably the Mu'tazila) used to argue against the dualists (الثنوية) that good and evil can come from one source (God). But then they themselves ended up ascribing one of the two aspects (good or evil) away from God, effectively agreeing with the dualists. Next, Maturidi addresses the objection about God creating something not beneficial. The opponent's answer (involving the burdening of the disabled) is shown to be false because it is not supported by the witnessed world. The opponent's claim of 'بديهة العقل' is exposed as a lie; he only uses analogy where it suits him. Then Maturidi shows that the opponent's own reasoning proves something true in the unseen but false in the witnessed, invalidating his method. Finally, Maturidi argues that ugliness is not intrinsic: even abominations and disbelief are not ugly in themselves because God could create such actions without being described as foul. What is considered wisdom from the aspect of benefit is harm from another aspect. Thus, moral judgments depend on context and divine decree, not on inherent qualities.

Key Terms
ثنوية
جواز
إحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that arguing against dualism is straightforward and consistent.
Correction: Maturidi shows that a seemingly valid argument against dualism can inadvertently endorse it if not carefully reasoned.

Checkpoint
What was the original purpose of the opponents' argument about good and evil coming from one source?
A) To prove dualism
B) To refute the belief in two principles
C) To deny the existence of evil
D) To affirm the intuition of reason

Key Takeaway
Even well-intentioned arguments can backfire if they abandon their own premises, and Maturidi exposes this inconsistency in his opponents' reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself arguing against a belief, only to realize later that you were actually assuming a key premise of that belief? How does Maturidi's exposure of inconsistency challenge you to examine your own arguments for hidden assumptions?
?
When you face evil or suffering in your life, do you ever implicitly attribute it to a source other than Allah—such as fate, people, or your own circumstances—even while verbally affirming that Allah alone is the Creator? What would it cost you to fully acknowledge that all good and evil come from Allah?
?
Maturidi’s opponents thought they were defending tawhid by denying evil from Allah, but they actually fell into dualism. Have you ever defended a truth in a way that undermined it? What is one specific way you can commit to being more intellectually consistent in your faith?

Critique of Claimed Intuition of Reason
Maturidi refutes the opponents' appeal to intuitive reason as a methodological fallacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the opponents' appeal to 'intuition of reason' (بديهة العقل) as a flawed argument based on selective analogy.

Core Idea
Claiming 'common sense' is often a cover for biased analogy.

After exposing the dualism inconsistency, Maturidi turns to a methodological critique. His opponents had used the notion of 'بديهة العقل' (intuition of reason) to support their claims. Maturidi argues that this appeal is fraudulent: it is merely an analogy that only holds when the opponent agrees. This lesson unpacks that critique and its implications for theological argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the segment from 'ثم دفع ما عورض' to 'كذب في الشاهد'. It contains three moves: a defense against an objection, a charge of false intuition, and a demonstration of invalid analogy.
This is a tight argument; read each sentence multiple times.
2
Identify the objection and defense
Maturidi says 'ثم دفع ما عورض بخلق شيء لا ينتفع به بما لم يدل ذلك على تكليف الزمن'. What is the objection about creation of non-beneficial things, and how is it answered?
The answer is that creating something without benefit does not imply burdening the disabled.
3
Understand the charge about intuition of reason
Maturidi says: 'فهذا يبين أن الذي قال ببديهة العقل كذب'. He claims the opponent's appeal to intuition is a lie.
Why is he so harsh? Because the opponent only applies intuition selectively.
4
Examine the false analogy
The next sentence: 'ثم حصل بما عارض به خصمه ... مما هو كذب في الشاهد'. The opponent's reasoning produces something true in the unseen but false in the witnessed world.
This invalidates the universal claim of intuition.

Source Text

Then their leaders continued to speak to the dualists about the permissibility of good and evil, pleasant and foul coming from one source, in order to refute the belief in two principles. Then they turned to ascribing one of the two aspects away from God. So whoever verified the other aspect in what exists in the world has made necessary what the dualists said. And there is no power except by Allah. Then he repelled the objection regarding the creation of something not beneficial by saying that does not indicate the burdening of the disabled. This shows that the one who spoke by the intuition of reason lied, and that he only claimed analogy on what his opponent agreed with him upon, nothing else. Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's final refutation of his opponents' arguments. He recounts how the opponents (probably the Mu'tazila) used to argue against the dualists (الثنوية) that good and evil can come from one source (God). But then they themselves ended up ascribing one of the two aspects (good or evil) away from God, effectively agreeing with the dualists. The opponent's claim of 'بديهة العقل' is exposed as a lie; he only uses analogy where it suits him. Finally, Maturidi argues that ugliness is not intrinsic: even abominations and disbelief are not ugly in themselves because God could create such actions without being described as foul. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that 'common sense' or 'intuition' is a universally valid source of knowledge.
Correction: Maturidi shows that such claims often mask biased reasoning that only works when the opponent agrees.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about the person who claims 'بديهة العقل'?
A) He is truthful and reliable
B) He is lying
C) He is using revelation
D) He is a dualist

Key Takeaway
An appeal to 'common sense' is no substitute for consistent reasoning; Maturidi exposes it as a form of special pleading.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim something is 'just common sense', are you sure you are not simply insisting on an idea because it suits your desires? Have you ever used an argument in one situation that you would reject in another? What does that reveal about your sincerity in seeking truth?
?
Maturidi exposes the opponent's lie. Consider your own life: where are you arguing from convenience rather than consistency? Are you willing to apply the same standard to yourself that you demand of others? What would change if you did?
?
The lesson shows that what seems intuitive in the unseen can be false in the witnessed. What beliefs do you hold about Allah or the unseen that you have never tested against the witnessed realities of justice and wisdom? Are you truly reasoning from the same principles, or are you protecting a comfortable assumption?

Ugliness is Not Intrinsic: Moral Relativity
Maturidi concludes that ugliness is not intrinsic by analyzing benefit, praiseworthiness, and possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that ugliness, abominations, and disbelief are not inherently evil; their moral status depends on divine attribution and context.

Core Idea
Evil and ugliness are not intrinsic; they depend on divine determination.

In the final part of this passage, Maturidi delivers the key conclusion: what is considered ugly or evil is not so by its own nature. He builds on the previous critique to show that even acts like abominations and disbelief are not inherently repugnant—they become so only by divine commandment or prohibition. This lesson explores that revolutionary idea and its implications for understanding good and evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the culminating argument
Read from 'ثم أجاب بما يوجد احتراز النفع' to the end. It contains two parallel arguments and a concluding nuance about wisdom and harm.
Notice how Maturidi uses examples from human actions to illustrate his point.
2
First argument: avoiding benefit is not praiseworthy
Maturidi says that if someone avoids benefit in an action, they are not praised. Therefore, what is considered ugly from God is not ugly in itself.
He is drawing an analogy between human and divine actions.
3
Second argument: abominations and disbelief
The opponent can commit abominations and disbelief, but they are not commanded to do so. Thus, those acts are not ugly in themselves.
This shows that ugliness is not a property of the act, but of the command.
4
Final nuance: wisdom and harm
The passage ends by distinguishing between two aspects: what is wisdom from one side may be harm from another. This relativizes moral categories.
This reinforces that moral judgments are not absolute but context-dependent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many believe that certain actions are inherently evil or ugly (e.g., lying, killing) regardless of context.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why are abominations and disbelief not ugly in themselves?
A) Because they are impossible for humans
B) Because they are not commanded
C) Because they are beneficial
D) Because they are from God

Key Takeaway
Moral categories like ugliness and evil are not inherent in actions but are determined by divine command and context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a sin you struggle with. If ugliness is not intrinsic but arises from God's command, does that change how you view your disobedience? Are you merely avoiding an 'ugly' act, or are you rejecting the authority of the One who commanded?
?
What would it mean for you to live as if every action's moral weight comes from God's command and not from your own assessment? Does that thought make you more or less accountable?
?
When you see evil in the world, do you instinctively judge it as 'ugly in itself'? How does Maturidi's argument challenge you to trust in God's wisdom even when you cannot see the benefit or harm behind His decree?

Refuting the Mu'tazila on Divine Will
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila position that God's will is nothing but His creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the Mu'tazila claim that God's will is identical to His creation, and why he considers their position self-contradictory.

Core Idea
Maturidi exposes the Mu'tazila's denial of divine will as inconsistent with their own admissions.

Maturidi now turns to a specific sectarian dispute with the Mu'tazila over the nature of God's will. The Mu'tazila claim that God's will is nothing other than His act of creation, denying it as a distinct attribute. In this passage, Maturidi highlights the inconsistency in their position by showing that the very meaning they assign to God's will—that He is not overpowered or compelled—is something they already admit in human actions. This lesson unpacks his logical refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazila position
The passage begins by stating the principle relied upon by the Mu'tazila: that God's will is nothing other than His creation. Read carefully to understand their claim and al-Ka'bi's interpretation.
Note that al-Ka'bi interprets God's will as the fact that God is not overpowered or compelled in His action.
2
Notice Maturidi's point of agreement
Maturidi observes that the Mu'tazila have already granted a similar meaning—that an agent is not overpowered or compelled—in the case of human actions.
3
Grasp the inconsistency
Since the Mu'tazila have granted this meaning generally for all actions, their denial of a separate divine will becomes meaningless. This is the core of Maturidi's refutation.
Maturidi concludes that their position reduces to an empty verbal dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل الذي هو معتمد المعتزلة أن إرادة الله ليست غير خلقه وأن تأويلها على ما فسر الكعبي ليس غير أنه لم يغلب ولم يضطر في فعله. وهذا المعنى قد أعطوه جميعاً في فعل العباد، فإنكارهم الإرادة - وهذا معناها - لا معنى له بعد الإعطاء في الجملة. والله الموفق.
Then the principle relied upon by the Mu'tazila is that God's will is nothing other than His creation, and its interpretation according to what al-Ka'bi interpreted is nothing other than that He is not overpowered nor compelled in His action. And this meaning they have all granted in the case of the actions of servants, so their denial of will – and this is its meaning – has no meaning after granting it in general. And God is the Granter of success.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage identifies the foundational principle (al-aṣl) of the Mu'tazila regarding God's will: they equate God's will with His act of creation, denying a separate attribute of will. Their leading theologian al-Ka'bi interpreted God's will as merely the fact that God is not overpowered (lam yughlab) nor compelled (lam yuḍṭarr) in His actions. Maturidi points out that this very meaning – that an agent is not overpowered or compelled – is something that even the Mu'tazila admit applies to human actions. Since they grant that human actions have this quality, their denial of a separate will for God becomes inconsistent. Thus, their position reduces to an empty verbal dispute.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الكعبي
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi is denying that God's will is distinct from His essence.
Correction: Maturidi is not denying distinct attributes; he is refuting the Mu'tazila's specific claim that will is nothing but creation.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the Mu'tazila interpretation of God's will (as per al-Ka'bi) essentially reduce to?
A) God's will is identical to His knowledge.
B) God is not overpowered or compelled in His action.
C) God's will is a separate eternal attribute.
D) Human actions are not willed by God.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila by showing that their own admission in human actions undermines their denial of a distinct divine will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a theological position, do you ever admit a principle for yourself that you deny for God? How does this lesson expose that inconsistency in your own thinking?
?
Are you willing to have your beliefs about God challenged by logical argument, or do you resist when your assumptions are shown to be incoherent?
?
The Mu'tazila's denial of divine will stems from a fear of anthropomorphism. What fears drive you to deny aspects of God's nature that are affirmed in revelation?

The Mu'tazilite View of Divine Will and Its Refutation
Maturidi summarizes the Mu'tazilite equation of will with creation and points out its inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite claim that God's will is identical to His creation, and Maturidi's critique that this leads to inconsistency because they affirm the same meaning of will for human actions.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites reduce divine will to creation while accepting its meaning for humans.

Maturidi here exposes a logical flaw in the Mu'tazilite position on divine will. He summarizes their view and then shows how they contradict themselves when they consider human actions. This lesson walks through his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the author's words clearly.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
Notice what the Mu'tazila say: God's will is nothing other than His creation. Also note al-Ka'bi's interpretation: it means God is not overcome or compelled.
This is the principle they rely on.
3
Follow the inconsistency
Maturidi points out that the Mu'tazilites grant the same meaning (not being compelled) for human actions, yet they deny a distinct will for God. Ask yourself why this is contradictory.
If will means absence of compulsion, and they accept that for humans, they cannot deny it for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل الذي هو معتمد المعتزلة أن إرادة الله ليست غير خلقه وأن تأويلها على ما فسر الكعبي ليس غير أنه لم يغلب ولم يضطر في فعله. وهذا المعنى قد أعطوه جميعا في فعل العباد، فإنكارهم الإرادة - وهذا معناها - لا معنى له بعد الإعطاء في الجملة. والله الموفق.
Then the principle upon which the Mu'tazila rely is that God's will is nothing other than His creation, and that its interpretation according to what al-Ka'bi explained is nothing other than that He is not overcome and not compelled in His action. And this meaning they have all granted in the case of the actions of servants, so their denial of will—this being its meaning—has no meaning after granting it in general. And God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the Mu'tazilite position on divine will. Maturidi states their foundational claim: God's will is identical to His act of creation, and al-Ka'bi's interpretation reduces it to 'not being overcome or compelled'. Then Maturidi points out an inconsistency: the Mu'tazilites themselves apply this same meaning (absence of coercion) to human actions—a human's will is also understood as acting without external compulsion. Yet they deny that God has a distinct will (irādah) separate from His creative act. Maturidi argues that if they already accept the meaning (not being compelled) for humans, they have effectively admitted the reality of will (since that is what will means), so their denial of will for God is meaningless.

Key Terms
إرادة
خلق
الغلبة
الاضطرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilites deny the existence of divine will entirely.
Correction: They actually affirm a concept of will (absence of compulsion) but refuse to call it a distinct attribute of God, leading to inconsistency.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi see as contradictory in the Mu'tazilite position on will?
A) They deny will for God but affirm it for humans with the same meaning
B) They claim God's will is eternal but human will is created
C) They say will is identical to knowledge
D) They affirm God's will but deny its efficacy

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite reduction of divine will to creation leads to a contradiction because they grant the same meaning of will to humans yet deny it for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever reduced an attribute of Allah to something less than what He has revealed, thinking it more 'reasonable'? What might you be sacrificing in doing so?
?
The Mu'tazilites accepted the meaning of will for humans but denied it for Allah. Are there areas in your life where you accept a truth about yourself but deny it about Allah, fearing anthropomorphism?
?
If Allah's will is real and distinct, how should that change the way you pray, trust, and submit to Him today? What one step will you take to live in light of His will being sovereign?

Al-Ka'bi's Misinterpretation of the Muslim Slogan
Maturidi refutes al-Ka'bi's reinterpretation of the Muslim slogan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi critiques al-Ka'bi's attempt to reinterpret 'Mā Shā'a Allāh Kāna' by separating will from praise and the two-aspect analysis.

Core Idea
Al-Ka'bi's reinterpretation leads to attributing error to God.

In this passage, Maturidi addresses al-Ka'bi's error in interpreting the common Muslim saying 'What God wills happens'. Al-Ka'bi tried to avoid praising God for willing insult by linking the saying to the verse 'Creator of everything'. Maturidi exposes two aspects of this reasoning, showing that it leads to ignorance and error.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of Maturidi's argument. Then read the translation carefully.
Pay attention to the two aspects Maturidi identifies.
2
Identify al-Ka'bi's error
Al-Ka'bi claims that the slogan implies no praise for God because 'willing insult' is not praiseworthy. Notice how he links it to the verse 'Creator of everything'.
3
Understand the two-aspect analysis
Maturidi distinguishes between willing to be a liar (ignorance) and willing the ugly act as ugly (wisdom). This shows al-Ka'bi conflates two different objects of will.
First aspect leads to error, second to wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر وهم الكعبي فيما ذكر في هذا الباب. سأل نفسه عن قول الناس: «ما شاء الله كان وما لا يشاء لا يكون»، فأجاب بالذي في تأويل قوله {خالق كل شيء} [الأنعام: 102]، أنه ليس في إرادة الشتم له مدح؛ وقد بينا ذلك. على أنه في إرادته أن يكون كاذبا فيما أخبر به حقيقة الشتم، وليس في إرادة كون فعل الشتم ممن يشتمه قبيحا شتم. يدل على ذلك العلم به في الوجهين: أنه في الأول جهل وخطأ، وفي الثاني حكمة وصواب. وصرف المشيئة إلى القهر، وقد بينا وهمه. على أن معنى القهر في هذا أو في غيره محال، لأنها هي في الإيمان والكفر والكذب والصدق، وهو لو خلق الكفر والكذب لا عن أحد في الحقيقة يكون كافرا كاذبا عند جميع من يرى خلق الشيء ذلك الشيء، فذلك يلزمهم أن يقولوا: تأويل قول المسلمين {ما شاء الله كان}، أي لو شاء لكفر وكذب. وهذا التأويل مما لا يرضى به مجنون أن يوجه إليه قوله، فكيف جملة المسلمين. وبالله التوفيق. ثم خرج من المعارضة بأخرى يقول بها على المسلمين فقال: هم يقولون {ما أحب الله كان وما لم يحب لا}؛ وهذا لم يسمع من شيطان، فكيف من مسلم.
Then we mention the error of al-Ka'bi concerning what he mentioned in this chapter. He asked himself about the people's saying: 'What God wills happens, and what He does not will does not happen,' and he answered by that which is in the interpretation of His saying {Creator of everything} [Al-An'am 6:102]: that there is no praise in the willing of insult (shatm) for Him; and we have clarified that. But in His willing to be a liar regarding what He informed about the reality of insult, there is no insult in willing the occurrence of the act of insult from the one who insults it as something ugly. The knowledge of that in two aspects indicates: in the first it is ignorance and error, and in the second it is wisdom and correctness. And he turned mashī'ah into compulsion (qahr), and we have clarified his error. Indeed the meaning of compulsion in this or other contexts is impossible, because it [mashī'ah] applies to faith and disbelief, lying and truthfulness. And if He created disbelief and lying from no one in reality, He would be an unbeliever and a liar according to all who consider creation of a thing as that thing. That would compel them to say: The interpretation of the Muslims' saying {What God wills happens} is: if He willed, He would disbelieve and lie. And this interpretation is such that even a madman would not be pleased to direct his saying to it, how then the whole community of Muslims? And with God is success. Then he came out of the opposition with another saying that he attributes to the Muslims: they say {What God loves happens, and what He does not love does not happen}; and this has not been heard from a devil, how then from a Muslim?
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ka'bi's interpretation of the common Muslim statement 'What God wills happens'. Maturidi reports that al-Ka'bi, when asked about this saying, interprets it by the verse 'Creator of everything' to avoid praising God for willing evil. Maturidi points out two aspects: if God willed to be a liar about the reality of insult, that would be ignorance and error, but willing the ugly act as ugly is wisdom. Al-Ka'bi reduces will to compulsion (qahr), which Maturidi dismisses as impossible because will applies to opposites (faith/disbelief). If God created disbelief and lying without a subject, He would Himself be attributed with disbelief and lying according to those who equate creation with the thing created. This leads to the absurd consequence that 'what God wills happens' means God could disbelieve and lie—an interpretation no Muslim would accept. Finally, al-Ka'bi invents another saying attributed to Muslims—'What God loves happens'—to distract from the original, but Maturidi denies that Muslims ever say that.

Key Terms
مشيئة
شتم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think al-Ka'bi's reinterpretation preserves God's transcendence by avoiding praise for willing evil.
Correction: Maturidi shows it leads to attributing error to God (ignorance) in the first aspect, or is unnecessary in the second.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the first aspect of al-Ka'bi's error?
A) Willing insult is praise
B) Willing to be a liar is ignorance
C) Willing ugly act as ugly is error
D) Both aspects are wisdom

Key Takeaway
Al-Ka'bi's error lies in confusing the object of will: willing to lie is ignorance, but willing the act as ugly is wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see evil in the world, do you ever secretly think that God's will for such evil is incompatible with His perfection? How does Maturidi's distinction between willing to be a liar and willing the act as ugly challenge your assumption? Will you now submit to God's wisdom even when you don't understand His decree?
?
Al-Ka'bi tried to change the meaning of the Muslim slogan to protect God's honor. Have you ever twisted a clear teaching of Islam because it made you uncomfortable? Are you willing to accept God's words as they are, trusting His wisdom over your own reasoning? What step will you take today to align your heart with the truth, even if it feels difficult?
?
Maturidi says that God willing an ugly act as something ugly is wisdom and correctness. In your own life, when you experience pain or injustice, do you blame God or believe that He is working with wise purpose? How can this lesson transform your reaction to trials—will you begin to see them as part of God's perfect plan and respond with patience and trust?

The Absurd Consequence of Reducing Will to Compulsion
Maturidi refutes reduction of will to compulsion and the invented saying.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects al-Ka'bi's reduction of will to compulsion and the absurd consequence it entails.

Core Idea
Reducing will to compulsion leads to attributing disbelief and lying to God.

Maturidi continues his refutation by addressing al-Ka'bi's move to turn will (mashī'ah) into compulsion (qahr). He argues that this is impossible because will applies to opposites like faith and disbelief. If God created disbelief without a subject, He would be considered an unbeliever, leading to an absurd interpretation of the Muslim slogan. Maturidi also dismisses al-Ka'bi's invented saying about love.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic from 'وصرف المشيئة إلى القهر' to the end, and the corresponding translation.
Focus on the argument about compulsion and the invented saying.
2
Understand the reduction to compulsion
Al-Ka'bi reinterprets 'will' as 'compulsion' (qahr). Maturidi says this is impossible because will applies to all opposites (faith/disbelief, lying/truth).
3
Grasp the absurd consequence
If God created disbelief from no one, then according to those who equate creation with the thing created, God Himself would be a disbeliever and liar. This would mean the slogan implies God could disbelieve and lie.
This consequence is unacceptable to any Muslim.
4
Recognize the invented saying
Al-Ka'bi then invents another saying attributed to Muslims: 'What God loves happens', to divert from the original. Maturidi denies that Muslims ever say this.

Source Text

He asked himself about the people's saying: 'What God wills happens, and what He does not will does not happen,' and he answered by that which is in the interpretation of His saying {Creator of everything} [Al-An'am 6:102]: that there is no praise in the willing of insult (shatm) for Him; and we have clarified that. But in His willing to be a liar regarding what He informed about the reality of insult, there is no insult in willing the occurrence of the act of insult from the one who insults it as something ugly. And if He created disbelief and lying from no one in reality, He would be an unbeliever and a liar according to all who consider creation of a thing as that thing. That would compel them to say: The interpretation of the Muslims' saying {What God wills happens} is: if He willed, He would disbelieve and lie. And this interpretation is such that even a madman would not be pleased to direct his saying to it, how then the whole community of Muslims? And with God is success. Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Ka'bi's interpretation of the common Muslim statement 'What God wills happens'. Misconception: One might think that reducing will to compulsion avoids attributing evil to God by making evil a matter of compulsion rather than will.
Correction: Maturidi shows it actually attributes evil to God, because compulsion still makes God the direct agent, resulting in God being a disbeliever and liar.

A) Only an unbeliever
B) Only a liar

Reducing divine will to compulsion leads to the absurd conclusion that God's will could entail His own disbelief and lying.

?

?

?
The passage warns against attributing to God what does not befit Him. Are there any everyday thoughts or words you use that unintentionally lower your perception of God? How can you purify your tongue and heart?

God's Power Is Absolute Over All Acts
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila claim using a hypothetical argument about God's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila claim that God cannot create human actions by showing that God's power is absolute and not comparable to human ability.

Core Idea
God's power is not limited to creating only what humans perceive as possible.

Imam Maturidi begins his refutation by considering a hypothetical: if God willed to create an act unlike any human act, He could do so—the fact that such acts are not observed does not prove inability. This sets the stage for dismantling the Mu'tazila position that human actions are created by humans themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the hypothetical scenario Maturidi presents.
The passage uses a conditional 'لو شاء' to set up the argument.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim
Understand that the Mu'tazila claim God cannot create human actions because if He willed them they would happen, but they do not. Maturidi points out that this is based on a flawed analogy with human power.
3
Analyze Maturidi's counterargument
Maturidi argues that God's power is not limited to creating acts that are similar to human acts. The creation of heavens and earth shows His absolute power.
This refutes the Mu'tazila claim by showing their assumption is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن الله لو شاء أن يخلق فعلاً ليس بفعل للخلق لا يقدر عليه حتى يجيئ الكتاب بالامتداح به والاقتدار عليه، وإنما قدر ذلك من الفعل في غيره مما ظهر من فعل آخر، وهو مما لا يبلغه حد البشر. فمن كان يظن أن الله يعجز عن هذا النوع من الخلق، فهو لا يقبل قدرة الله على حقيقة فعل الخلق؛ بل لو أريد ذا لكان موضعه فيما ظن المعتزلة أنهم خلقوا خلقاً وليس في العقول أرفع منه ولا أعلى في الحسن والفضل. فرمت هذا المعتزلة على ألسن الضعفة، فبين الله قدرته على مثل ذلك، وإلا لا وجه لإنكار مثله ممن يُقر له بخلق السموات والأرض وما بينهما. ولكن بين بذلك فساد قول المعتزلة: إن الله قد شاء فلم يكن، إذ هو لا يقدر على خلق أفعال العباد، فقال: وهو على كل شيء قدير (المائدة: 120) جوابًا لذلك، وقال تعالى: فلو شاء لهداكم أجمعين (الأنعام: 149) جوابًا للأول. وقوله: ولو يشاء الله لانتصر منهم (محمد: 4) على أنه لو شاء تعذيب منذريه، بل لو شاء لانتصر منهم بما شاء، ولكن شاء التأخير. والثاني لانتصر منهم بهم، ولكن شاء أن يبلو صحابة نبيه بالهزيمة ليتبين الذين صدقوا كقوله تعالى: ولقد فتنا الذين من قبلهم، وقوله: ومن الناس من يعبد الله على حرف.
If God had willed to create an act that is not an act of the creation, He would not be able to do it unless the Book came with praise of it and power over it. But He only empowered that act in other than it, from what appeared from another act, and it is something that the limit of humans does not reach. So whoever thinks that God is incapable of this type of creation, he does not accept God's power over the reality of creating an act; rather, if this were intended, its place would be in what the Mu'tazila thought that they created creation, and in the intellects there is nothing higher nor greater in goodness and virtue. So the Mu'tazila threw this on the tongues of the weak, so God showed His power over the like of that, otherwise there would be no reason for denying the like of it from one who acknowledges to Him the creation of the heavens and the earth and what is between them. But He made clear through that the corruption of the Mu'tazila's saying: 'God willed but it did not happen, because He is not able to create the actions of servants.' So He said: 'And He is over all things competent' (Quran 5:120) as an answer to that. And He said: 'So if He willed, He would have guided you all' (Quran 6:149) as an answer to the first. And His saying: 'And if God willed, He would have taken vengeance upon them' (Quran 47:4) indicating that if He willed to punish those who warn, rather if He willed He would take vengeance upon them by whatever He willed, but He willed delay. And the second interpretation: He would take vengeance upon them by them, but He willed to try the companions of His Prophet with defeat to make clear those who are truthful, like His saying: 'And We have certainly tried those before them' (Quran 29:3) and His saying: 'And among the people is he who worships God on an edge' (Quran 22:11).
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila who claim that God cannot create human actions because if He willed them they would happen, but they do not. Maturidi argues that God's power is absolute and not comparable to human power. He says that if God had willed to create an act that is unlike human acts, He could do so; the fact that such acts are not observed does not indicate inability. The Mu'tazila's claim that they themselves create their acts is arrogance. God demonstrates His power by creating the heavens and earth. The verses from the Quran prove that God has power over all things, including guiding all people or taking vengeance. The delay in punishment does not imply inability. Thus the Mu'tazila argument is corrupt because it denies God's power based on human analogy.

Key Terms
قدرة
خلق
أفعال العباد
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some hold that because humans have free will, God cannot create their actions.
Correction: Maturidi shows that God's power is absolute and not limited by human agency; creation of human actions does not negate free will.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the flaw in the Mu'tazila claim that God cannot create human actions?
A) They assume God's power is like human power.
B) They deny the creation of heavens and earth.
C) They claim humans create their own actions.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
God's power is not limited by human analogy; He is capable of creating all actions, including human acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every action you take—even the ones you feel most proud or ashamed of—is within God's absolute power? How does that affect your sense of responsibility and your reliance on Him?
?
When you sin, do you ever secretly think God was unable to prevent it, as if your will escaped His control? How does this lesson challenge that hidden assumption, and what would it mean to fully surrender that belief?
?
If God created even your good deeds, does that make gratitude harder or more natural? And if He created your bad deeds, does that push you toward deeper repentance or toward blaming Him? Examine your heart honestly.

Quranic Proofs of God's Absolute Power
Maturidi cites Quranic verses to refute the Mu'tazila claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses Quranic verses to prove that God has full power over all things, including human actions.

Core Idea
The Quran explicitly affirms that God is over all things competent.

After presenting the logical argument, Maturidi cites several Quranic verses to show that the claim of the Mu'tazila contradicts divine revelation. These verses demonstrate that God's will and power are absolute, and any apparent delay in punishment or guidance does not indicate inability.

Guided Path
1
Read the verses cited
Read the Arabic passage containing the verses: 5:120, 6:149, 47:4, and 29:3, 22:11. Notice how each verse addresses a different aspect of God's power.
The verses are used as direct counterarguments to Mu'tazila claims.
2
Understand each verse's relevance
Each verse responds to a specific Mu'tazila objection. For example, the claim that 'God willed but it did not happen' is refuted by 5:120 which declares God's power over all things.
3
Synthesize the argument
Maturidi shows that the Mu'tazila position is internally inconsistent because the Quran repeatedly affirms God's ability to do what He wills.
The delay in punishment is a matter of divine wisdom, not inability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن الله لو شاء أن يخلق فعلاً ليس بفعل للخلق لا يقدر عليه حتى يجيئ الكتاب بالامتداح به والاقتدار عليه، وإنما قدر ذلك من الفعل في غيره مما ظهر من فعل آخر، وهو مما لا يبلغه حد البشر. فمن كان يظن أن الله يعجز عن هذا النوع من الخلق، فهو لا يقبل قدرة الله على حقيقة فعل الخلق؛ بل لو أريد ذا لكان موضعه فيما ظن المعتزلة أنهم خلقوا خلقاً وليس في العقول أرفع منه ولا أعلى في الحسن والفضل. فرمت هذا المعتزلة على ألسن الضعفة، فبين الله قدرته على مثل ذلك، وإلا لا وجه لإنكار مثله ممن يُقر له بخلق السموات والأرض وما بينهما. ولكن بين بذلك فساد قول المعتزلة: إن الله قد شاء فلم يكن، إذ هو لا يقدر على خلق أفعال العباد، فقال: وهو على كل شيء قدير (المائدة: 120) جوابًا لذلك، وقال تعالى: فلو شاء لهداكم أجمعين (الأنعام: 149) جوابًا للأول. وقوله: ولو يشاء الله لانتصر منهم (محمد: 4) على أنه لو شاء تعذيب منذريه، بل لو شاء لانتصر منهم بما شاء، ولكن شاء التأخير. والثاني لانتصر منهم بهم، ولكن شاء أن يبلو صحابة نبيه بالهزيمة ليتبين الذين صدقوا كقوله تعالى: ولقد فتنا الذين من قبلهم، وقوله: ومن الناس من يعبد الله على حرف.
If God had willed to create an act that is not an act of the creation, He would not be able to do it unless the Book came with praise of it and power over it. But He only empowered that act in other than it, from what appeared from another act, and it is something that the limit of humans does not reach. So whoever thinks that God is incapable of this type of creation, he does not accept God's power over the reality of creating an act; rather, if this were intended, its place would be in what the Mu'tazila thought that they created creation, and in the intellects there is nothing higher nor greater in goodness and virtue. So the Mu'tazila threw this on the tongues of the weak, so God showed His power over the like of that, otherwise there would be no reason for denying the like of it from one who acknowledges to Him the creation of the heavens and the earth and what is between them. But He made clear through that the corruption of the Mu'tazila's saying: 'God willed but it did not happen, because He is not able to create the actions of servants.' So He said: 'And He is over all things competent' (Quran 5:120) as an answer to that. And He said: 'So if He willed, He would have guided you all' (Quran 6:149) as an answer to the first. And His saying: 'And if God willed, He would have taken vengeance upon them' (Quran 47:4) indicating that if He willed to punish those who warn, rather if He willed He would take vengeance upon them by whatever He willed, but He willed delay. And the second interpretation: He would take vengeance upon them by them, but He willed to try the companions of His Prophet with defeat to make clear those who are truthful, like His saying: 'And We have certainly tried those before them' (Quran 29:3) and His saying: 'And among the people is he who worships God on an edge' (Quran 22:11).
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila who claim that God cannot create human actions because if He willed them they would happen, but they do not. Maturidi argues that God's power is absolute and not comparable to human power. He says that if God had willed to create an act that is unlike human acts, He could do so; the fact that such acts are not observed does not indicate inability. The Mu'tazila's claim that they themselves create their acts is arrogance. God demonstrates His power by creating the heavens and earth. The verses from the Quran prove that God has power over all things, including guiding all people or taking vengeance. The delay in punishment does not imply inability. Thus the Mu'tazila argument is corrupt because it denies God's power based on human analogy.

Key Terms
قدرة
خلق
أفعال العباد
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God willed something and it did not happen, it means He is not able.
Correction: Maturidi shows that God's will is always executed according to His wisdom; what appears as 'not happening' is actually part of a larger divine plan.

Checkpoint
Which verse does Maturidi cite to directly refute the claim that God cannot create human actions?
A) Quran 5:120
B) Quran 6:149
C) Quran 47:4
D) Quran 29:3

Key Takeaway
The Quran repeatedly affirms God's absolute power, and any apparent contradiction is due to divine wisdom, not impotence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if God is truly 'over all things competent'? When you worry about your future, your actions, or the state of the world, do you implicitly assume that some things are beyond His control? How would your daily anxiety change if you fully internalized this verse?
?
When you see punishment delayed—whether for yourself or others—do you interpret it as God's inability to act, or do you trust that His delay is deliberate and wise, even when you cannot see the reason? What does your patience (or lack thereof) reveal about your belief in His absolute power?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed God cannot create human actions. In what areas of your life do you subtly think your choices are outside God's control? Consider your habits, your faith, your repentance. Are there any actions you assume are solely yours, independent of His will? How does the verse 'If He willed, He would have guided you all' challenge that assumption?

Attributing Good Deeds: Command or Gratitude?
Maturidi presents the agreement and first issue on attributing good deeds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Maturidi school distinguishes between attributing good deeds to Allah from the aspect of command versus from the aspect of gratitude, and why the latter is considered more appropriate.

Core Idea
Good deeds are attributed to Allah for gratitude, not just command.

Imam Maturidi begins by establishing an agreed-upon principle with the Mu'tazila: nothing that implies ugliness is attributed to Allah. From this principle, he derives the first issue concerning how good deeds are attributed. The Mu'tazila say it is by command; Maturidi argues it is by gratitude and praise. This lesson unpacks that debate and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style and the use of key terms like قبح, إضافة, شكر.
2
Identify the agreed principle
The first sentence states a point of agreement: nothing is attributed to Allah that would imply ugliness. Underline this foundational rule.
3
Contrast the two views
Find where the Mu'tazila view is presented (attribute from command) and the Maturidi response (attribute from gratitude). Compare the reasoning.
The Maturidi view is not a rejection but a refinement.
4
Examine the evidence
The passage uses the example of faith as a blessing from Allah. Note how this clarifies the Maturidi position: attribution is about thanks, not just command.
This connects to the core idea.
5
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why the Maturidi view is 'more appropriate' (أولى). The key is that command is shared by believers and disbelievers, but gratitude distinguishes them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نتفق والمعتزلة [على] أن الله تعالى لا يضاف إليه شيء من الخلق أو الأفعال إلا من الوجه الذي لا يوهم القبح في الأسماء، وما يوهم ذلك فحقه أن يُنفي عنه ذلك. ويُخرج على هذا مسائل. إحداها في وجه إضافة ما أضيف إلى الله من الخيرات أنها من الله . قالت المعتزلة تضاف إليه من حيث أمر [بها] ودعا إليها وقوي عليها. وقلنا نحن هذا من الإضافة وإن كان حسنا فلا هذا يراد بالإضافة إليه عند ذكر الأفعال، ولكن المراد الشكر والحمد له إذا ذكرت الأفعال. وقد يجوز الأول، وهذا أولى، لأنه من حيث الأمر والدعاء والتقوية اشترك فيه المؤمن والكافر، ومن جهة الشكر والحمد يختلف. ومما يبين ذلك جواز القول المطلق إن الإيمان من نعم الله ومننه، وإن المؤمن قد أنعم الله عليه ومَنْ، وأنه لولا فضل الله عليه ما زُكِّي ، ولَمَسّه عذاب عظيم . ومن هذا الوجه لا يضاف إلى الله في الكافر، وإذا لم تذكر الأفعال فعلى الأمر. والله الموفق.
Then we and the Mu'tazila agree that nothing of creation or actions is attributed to Allah Almighty except from the aspect that does not imply ugliness in names, and whatever implies that, its due is that it be denied of Him. And upon this are derived issues. One of them is concerning the aspect of attributing what is attributed to Allah of good things, that they are from Allah. The Mu'tazila said: They are attributed to Him from the aspect that He commanded them, called to them, and strengthened them. And we said: This attribution, even if it is good, is not intended by attribution to Him when actions are mentioned; rather, what is intended is gratitude and praise to Him when actions are mentioned. And the first is permissible, but this is more appropriate, because from the aspect of command, call, and strengthening, the believer and disbeliever share in it, but from the aspect of gratitude and praise they differ. And among what clarifies that is the permissibility of the absolute statement that faith is among the blessings and favors of Allah, and that Allah has bestowed upon the believer and favored him, and that were it not for Allah's grace upon him, he would not have been purified, and a great punishment would have touched him. And from this aspect it is not attributed to Allah in the case of the disbeliever. And when actions are not mentioned, then it is from the aspect of command. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage begins by stating a point of agreement between the Maturidis and the Mu'tazila: nothing is attributed to Allah that would imply ugliness in names. Whatever implies ugliness must be negated from Him. From this principle, three issues (masā'il) are derived. The first issue is about the manner of attributing good things to Allah. The Mu'tazila hold that good things are attributed to Allah because He commanded them, called to them, and gave strength to perform them. The Maturidis respond that although this form of attribution is good, it is not what is intended when actions are mentioned in the context of attribution to Allah. Instead, the intended meaning is gratitude and praise to Allah. The first interpretation (the Mu'tazilite one) is permissible, but the Maturidi interpretation is more appropriate. The reason is that command, call, and strengthening are common to both the believer and the disbeliever (i.e., both are commanded and given strength), while gratitude and praise differentiate them. The passage provides evidence: it is permissible to say that faith is a blessing from Allah and that Allah has favored the believer—such statements are not made about the disbeliever. Therefore, when actions are mentioned, attribution is from the aspect of thanks; when actions are not mentioned, attribution is from the aspect of command.

Key Terms
قبح
أسماء
إضافة
أمر
شكر
حمد
نعم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that attributing good deeds to Allah means He commanded them in the same way for all people.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the intended attribution for good deeds is for gratitude and praise, not merely command, because command is shared by all while gratitude distinguishes believers.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between the Mu'tazila and Maturidi on attributing good deeds to Allah?
A) Mu'tazila attribute from command; Maturidi attribute from thanks and praise
B) Mu'tazila deny attribution; Maturidi affirm
C) Maturidi attribute from command; Mu'tazila from thanks
D) Both agree it is from command

Key Takeaway
Good deeds are attributed to Allah primarily as objects of gratitude, distinguishing the believer's thanks from mere shared command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you feel genuine gratitude to Allah for enabling it, or do you simply acknowledge that He commanded it? Which feeling dominates your heart, and why does that matter?
?
The passage says that even faith itself is a blessing and favor from Allah, and without His grace you would not be purified. Do you truly live with this awareness, or do you secretly take credit for your own faith? What would change in your daily life if you fully internalized that your very belief is a continual gift from Him?

Actions Not Attributable from Command
Maturidi argues against attributing actions to Allah from command, using Quranic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why actions cannot be attributed to Allah from the perspective of command, and how command implies obligation and burden, which are not consistent with divine attribution.

Core Idea
Command implies obligation, a burden; Allah is praised for grace, not for imposing obligations.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi addresses a common error: attributing actions to Allah merely because He commands them. He explains that command entails obligation and hardship, which are not fitting for divine attribution. Instead, actions are related to Allah from the perspective of praise and gratitude, as shown in Quranic verses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the example of those who claimed a distorted book is from Allah.
The passage begins with a Quranic example of misguided attributions.
2
Identify the key principle
Find where the author states that action is not permissible from the aspect of command. Note why: command contains only obligation and great burdens.
This principle is the core of the passage.
3
Understand the Quranic evidence
Look at the two Quranic verses cited: 'But Allah bestows favor upon you' and 'Were it not for the grace of Allah...' How do they support the argument?
These verses highlight Allah's grace, not obligation.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Consider the difference between command (imposing obligations) and favor (bestowing grace). Why is one attributable and the other not?
This distinction safeguards divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا طعن الله قال بالكتاب المبدل أنه من عند الله وبإضافتهم البحيرة إليه من ونحوها؛ إنهم ادعوا الأمر بذلك، فبرأ الله نفسه عن ذلك، وأخبر أن ذلك من عمل الشيطان، وأنهم قالوا ذلك حسدًا من عند أنفسهم . ولا قوة إلا بالله. ولا يجوز أيضاً الفعل من حيث الأمر ، لأنه ليس فيه إلا إلزام، وفي ذلك مؤن عظيمة لا يضاف إليه بذلك، بل من جهة الحمد والشكر كما قال: بل الله يمن عليكم ، الآية، وقال: فلولا فضل الله عليكم ورحمته ، الآية.
And for this reason Allah criticized those who said concerning the altered book that it is from Allah and their attribution of the bahira to Him and the like; they claimed command regarding that, so Allah declared Himself innocent of that, and informed that that is from the work of Satan, and that they said that out of envy from themselves. And there is no power except with Allah. And likewise, the action is not permissible from the aspect of command, because there is nothing in it except obligation, and in that are great burdens which are not attributed to Him by that; rather, from the aspect of praise and gratitude as He said: 'But Allah bestows favor upon you'—the verse, and He said: 'Were it not for the grace of Allah upon you and His mercy'—the verse.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage addresses the second issue: that actions cannot be attributed to Allah from the aspect of command. It begins with a Quranic example: Allah criticized those who claimed that a distorted book was from Allah and who attributed practices like the bahira (a pagan camel) to Him. They claimed that Allah commanded these things, but Allah declared Himself innocent of that and said it was from Satan and from their own envy. Then the author states the principle: the act from the aspect of command is not permissible because it involves nothing but obligation (ilzām), and that obligation entails great burdens (ma'ūn). Such burdens are not attributed to Allah from that aspect; rather, they are attributed from the aspect of praise and gratitude. This is supported by Quranic verses: 'But Allah bestows favor upon you' and 'Were it not for the grace of Allah upon you and His mercy.'

Key Terms
أمر
إلزام
مؤن
حمد
شكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because Allah commands actions, those actions are attributable to Him as commands.
Correction: This passage clarifies that command implies obligation and burden, which are not fitting for Allah. Actions are attributable to Allah from the aspect of praise and gratitude, not from command.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say actions cannot be attributed to Allah from the aspect of command?
A) Because command implies obligation, which involves hardship not attributable to Allah
B) Because Allah never commands anything
C) Because actions are not real
D) Because command is only for prophets

Key Takeaway
Actions are not attributable to Allah from command because command entails obligation and burden; rather, they are attributable from the aspect of praise and gratitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read about Allah's commands, do you ever feel a sense of burden that distances you from Him? How does this lesson challenge that feeling and invite you to see His actions as grace?
?
Are there areas in your life where you have attributed something to Allah that He has distanced Himself from—like blaming Him for your own mistakes or seeing His commands as harsh? What would it look like to repent from that and embrace His grace instead?
?
If Allah's actions toward you are from praise and gratitude, not obligation, how should that change the way you approach Him in worship? What is one concrete step you will take today to shift from viewing His commands as duties to seeing them as gifts?

Al-Ka'bi's View on Attributing Only Beautiful Acts to Allah
Maturidi critiques al-Ka'bi's claim that only beautiful acts are attributed to Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ka'bi's position that only beautiful acts are attributed to Allah and Maturidi's initial critique regarding the attribution of acts of obedience.

Core Idea
Al-Ka'bi's principle contradicts itself: command implies obligation, not beauty.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi engages with a view from al-Ka'bi, a prominent Mu'tazilite scholar. Al-Ka'bi asserts that only what is beautiful and good should be attributed to Allah. However, Maturidi immediately identifies a tension: how can acts of obedience, which are commanded, be considered beautiful? Let's examine the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two claims
Carefully read the Arabic and translation. Note al-Ka'bi's first claim that only beautiful acts are attributed to Allah, and his second claim that acts of obedience are attributed from the aspect of command.
Pay attention to the key terms: حسن جميل and أمر.
2
Analyze Maturidi's rhetorical question
Maturidi asks: 'And what beauty is in that?' referring to command. Consider why command might not be considered beautiful in the same sense as acts of worship.
Think about the implications of command involving obligation and hierarchy.
3
Reflect on the incomplete argument
The passage cuts off, but we can see the beginning of Maturidi's critique. Consider what problems he might have explained further.
The source notes the argument is incomplete; we must be careful not to overinterpret.
4
Consider the broader theological context
Al-Ka'bi was a Mu'tazilite. His view relates to divine justice and the nature of good and evil. Reflect on how this passage fits into the debate on divine attributes.
This lesson is a starting point for understanding the Mu'tazilite position and Maturidi's refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الكعبي : لا يضاف إلى الله إلا الحسن الجميل، ثم زعم في إضافة الطاعات إليه أنه من وجه الأمر. وأي حسن في ذلك؟ وقد بيّنا ما يدخل على ذلك. وزعم أنه لا تضاف اليه...
And al-Ka'bi said: Nothing is attributed to Allah except the beautiful and good. Then he claimed regarding the attribution of acts of obedience to Him that it is from the aspect of command. And what beauty is in that? And we have already explained what problems enter upon that. And he claimed that nothing is attributed to Him...
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage presents the view of al-Ka'bi (a prominent Mu'tazilite scholar). He states that only what is beautiful and good (al-ḥasan al-jamīl) is attributed to Allah. However, he then claims that acts of obedience (ṭā'āt) are attributed to Allah from the aspect of command. The author questions this: 'And what beauty is in that?'—pointing out that command involves obligation, which is not beautiful. The author says they have already explained the problems inherent in this position. The text then cuts off as al-Ka'bi's claim that nothing is attributed to Him (other than the beautiful) is interrupted.

Key Terms
الكعبي
حسن جميل
طاعات
أمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that al-Ka'bi's view is consistent: only beautiful acts are from Allah, and acts of obedience are beautiful because they are commanded.
Correction: Maturidi shows that command itself involves obligation and hierarchy, which is not inherently beautiful. The very act of commanding, when attributed to Allah, may not fit al-Ka'bi's criterion of beauty.

Checkpoint
What is al-Ka'bi's position as quoted in the passage?
A) Only ugly acts are attributed to Allah
B) Only beautiful acts are attributed to Allah, and acts of obedience are from the aspect of command
C) All acts are attributed equally
D) No acts are attributed to Allah

Key Takeaway
Al-Ka'bi's attempt to restrict divine attribution to the beautiful fails to account for the nature of command, which involves obligation and thus cannot be called beautiful in the same sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of obedience, do you see it primarily as a beautiful act or as an obligation? How does your perception affect the sincerity of your worship? Examine your recent deeds—how many did you perform out of a sense of duty rather than a desire for closeness to Allah's beauty? Are you willing to obey even when the command feels burdensome and lacks immediate appeal?
?
Al-Ka'bi wanted to attribute only beautiful acts to Allah, but Maturidi showed that command itself may not be beautiful. In your own life, have you ever rejected a divine command because it didn't seem 'beautiful' to you? How has that affected your relationship with Allah? Consider one command you struggle with—are you obeying it anyway, or do you let your sense of 'beauty' dictate your obedience?
?
If you genuinely believed that every command from Allah is beautiful, would your obedience change? Reflect on a time when you obeyed reluctantly. Was it because you saw the command as an imposition rather than a gift? Ask yourself: Is your faith in Allah's wisdom strong enough to obey Him even when the command seems harsh or incomprehensible? What would it take for you to see His commands as inherently beautiful?

The Consensus on Condemning the Name Qadariyya
Maturidi reports the consensus condemning the name Qadariyya and cites a hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the people of kalam unanimously condemned the name Qadariyya and that a prophetic hadith provides the criterion for who truly deserves this label.

Core Idea
The name Qadariyya is universally condemned and applies to deniers of divine predestination.

Imam Maturidi opens a new section on the condemnation of the Qadariyya (or Mu'tazila). He reports that there is a consensus among the scholars of kalam that the name 'Qadariyya' is blameworthy, and each scholar distances themselves from it. He then mentions a hadith that points to the real meaning of the term. This lesson examines this consensus and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the author's words stand.
Notice the strong language: 'ajma'a' (consensus) and 'tabarra'a' (disassociation).
2
Understand the consensus
Identify that the people of kalam (theologians) are unanimous in condemning the name Qadariyya. This is a collective judgment.
Consensus (ijma') is a strong proof in Islamic theology.
3
Note the disassociation
Each theologian dissociates from the name. This shows that the label is considered negative and avoided.
4
Reflect on the hadith
The hadith is mentioned as a means to know who truly deserves the name. It implies a criterion.
The exact hadith is not given here, but it is referenced.
5
Consider the common misunderstanding
Some might think Qadariyya refers to those who affirm qadr, but the text implies it refers to those who deny it.
This is a key clarification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في ذم القدرية أو المعتزلة قال الشيخ رحمه الله : أجمع أهل الكلام على ذم اسم القدرية، وتبرأ كل منهم عنه. وقد روى في ذلك عن رسول الله ما يمكن به السبيل إلى معرفة من له حقيقة هذا.
A question concerning the condemnation of the Qadariyya or the Mu'tazila. The Shaykh, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The people of kalam have unanimously agreed on condemning the name Qadariyya, and each one of them dissociates from it. And a hadith has been narrated concerning that from the Messenger of Allah that enables the way to know who truly deserves this [name].
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage is a short doctrinal note on the term 'Qadariyya'. The author presents a new section titled 'A question concerning the condemnation of the Qadariyya or the Mu'tazila'. He then reports the consensus (ijma') of the people of kalam (theologians) that the name 'Qadariyya' is condemned (dhamm), and each theologian dissociates himself from it. He further mentions that a hadith has been narrated from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) that provides the means to know who truly deserves this label. The implication is that the name is disgraced, and it is applied to those who deny divine predestination (qadr), not to those who affirm it.

Key Terms
أهل الكلام
ذم
القدرية
تبرأ
رسول الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the term Qadariyya refers to all who affirm qadr (predestination).
Correction: Actually, it refers to those who deny divine qadr, as the hadith indicates.

Checkpoint
What is the stance of the people of kalam toward the name Qadariyya?
A) They all accept it
B) They condemn it and each disassociates from it
C) They are indifferent
D) They praise it

Key Takeaway
The people of kalam unanimously condemned the name Qadariyya, and a prophetic hadith clarifies that it applies to deniers of predestination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Exposing inconsistency in Qadariyya's stance on evil
Maturidi exposes inconsistency in Qadariyya's stance on attribution of evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi exposes a logical inconsistency in the Qadariyya's position on the attribution of evil to God.

Core Idea
The Qadariyya contradict themselves when they deny evil from God but affirm misguidance from Him.

Maturidi presents a sharp logical critique of the Qadariyya sect. They claim that evils cannot be attributed to God because He forbade them. Yet they still attribute misguidance—which God also forbade—to Him. This inconsistency reveals the weakness of their position. In this lesson, we examine Maturidi's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the argument. Note the key terms: Qadariyya, evil, misguidance, error, deviation.
The argument is concise, so pay attention to each clause.
2
Identify the Qadariyya claim and the inconsistency
The Qadariyya say evil is not from God because He forbade it. But they still attribute misguidance to God, even though He also forbade it. Why do they allow that? Maturidi points out this self-contradiction.
This step is crucial for understanding the refutation.
3
Reflect on the broader implication
This refutation shows that the Qadariyya do not consistently apply their own principle. If prohibition were sufficient reason to deny attribution, then misguidance should also be denied.
Consider how this argument strengthens the Sunni position that all actions are from God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الهداية، بل كل شيء يقابل ما ذكر؛ إذ الأمر والتقوية كلاهما للمحنة وفيهما التخلية، فإذا استقام ذا، ولم يستقم الآخر بان أن في ذا معنى ليس في الآخر. مع ما زعمت القدرية أن الشرور لا تضاف إليه، لأنه نهى عنها، فقد نهى عن الضلال والغواية والزيغ، فلم أضيفت إليه؟ والله الموفق.
Guidance, rather everything corresponds to what was mentioned; for command and empowerment are both for trial and in them is desolation. So when this one is sound and the other is not sound, it becomes clear that in this one there is a meaning not in the other. Along with what the Qadariyya claim that evils are not attributed to Him because He forbade them; yet He forbade misguidance, error, and deviation, so why is it attributed to Him? And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage is a brief refutation pointing out an inconsistency in the Qadariyya position. Maturidi begins by noting that guidance and everything opposite are paired in a trial context, and if one is sound and the other not, there is a distinct meaning. Then he states: the Qadariyya claim that evil acts cannot be attributed to God because He has forbidden them; but they acknowledge that misguidance, error, and deviation—which God has also forbidden—are nevertheless attributed to God. This is a self-contradiction. The passage ends with a prayer for divine success.

A) They claim God creates both good and evil, but then deny evil.
B) They claim evil is not attributed to God because He forbade it, yet they attribute misguidance to Him even though He also forbade it.
C) They say God is not the creator of evil, but they accept that He creates punishment.
D) They deny that God has power over evil, but affirm that He has power over good.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi exposes that the Qadariyya's principle—that what God forbids cannot be attributed to Him—is inconsistently applied, since they still attribute misguidance to Him.

?

?
Maturidi shows that logic demands consistency. In your own understanding of God's role in evil, where have you made exceptions without realizing it? Will you today commit to a coherent view that honors God's transcendence without escaping responsibility?
