Refutation of Mu'tazila and Khawarij on Minor Sins
Maturidi refutes Mu'tazila and Khawarij on minor sins via a conditional argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the positions of the Mu'tazila and Khawarij regarding minor sins by arguing that requiring forgiveness or excommunication would imply injustice in God.

Core Idea
Extreme views on minor sins lead to attributing injustice to God.

This passage from Imam Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid addresses two extreme positions on minor sins. The Mu'tazila held that God must forgive minor sins, while the Khawarij held that any minor sin removes one from faith. Maturidi refutes both by showing they would imply divine injustice or limits on wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: a conditional statement followed by a conclusion.
2
Understand the conditional argument
The author presents a conditional: if minor sins were not subject to possible punishment in wisdom, then that would be transgression and injustice. This is meant to show that the Mu'tazila view negates God's wisdom.
The argument is subtle: it assumes that God's wisdom allows for punishment of minor sins, so requiring forgiveness would be limiting God.
3
Identify the two refuted positions
The passage explicitly states that this negates the Mu'tazila view of obligatory forgiveness for minor sins and the Khawarij view of removing faith. Both are shown to be problematic.
The phrase 'إزالة اسم الإيمان' refers to the Khawarij declaring someone a disbeliever for a minor sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سؤلهم . ولو لم تكن ذنوبهم بحيث احتمال التعذيب عليها في الحكمة، أو كان عليهم من ذلك خوفُ التعذيب لكان في ذلك تعدى الحد والوصف بالجور والتعدي منه، وذلك أعظم من الزلات. فهذا ينفي قول المعتزلة في إثبات المغفرة في الصغائر وإخراج فعل الحكمة، وقول الخوارج بإزالة اسم الإيمان عنه. ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم القول في جعل الصغائر كفرًا أو شركا، أو التخليد في النار جزاء لها قول مهجور بما يُسقط معنى تسميته عفواً غفورا رحيما، إذ لا يسعه مأثم ولا زلة بلا توبة؛ ويوجب به بعد أن عرفه عفواً غفورا كريما، وعادي لأجله من أزال عنه هذا الاسم إلى كل ما يوصف [به] كل قاس وكل لئيم؛ وبه يستحق الذي قال ، إذ هذا أعظم الذنوب حيث [بدل] صفات الرب. ثم بما بلى به الأنبياء ، فيكفّر بهم في تلك الأحوال؛ ومن كفّر بنبي في وقت فهو كافر لا ريب فيه . ثم بهذا وصف الربَّ بالجور لما فيه إبطال الحسنات بزلة، والعدل هو الذي يجزي بالإحسان والإساءة فيما أظهر عز وجل من كرمه؛ ثم [ب] التجهيل بما لم يعرف من يصلح للرسالة ويقوم بأداء الأمانة . ثم بما لا أحد [ خال] عنه، فيكون في الذي ذكر تكليف ما لا يطاق؛ ثم ينقطع منه الخوف والرجاء ويحصل الأمر على الأمن والإياس، وقد شهد عليهما بالضلال والكفر . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Their question. If their sins were not such that the probability of punishment upon them is in wisdom, or if they had fear of punishment from that, then in that would be transgression of the limit and description of injustice and transgression from Him, and that is greater than the lapses. So this negates the statement of the Mu'tazila in affirming forgiveness for minor sins and removing the act of wisdom, and the statement of the Khawarij in removing the name of faith from him. There is no power except with Allah. Then, the statement regarding making minor sins kufr or shirk, or eternal punishment in the Fire as recompense for them, is an abandoned statement, because it nullifies the meaning of His being called 'Pardoner', 'Forgiver', 'Merciful', since He cannot accommodate any offense or lapse without repentance; and it necessitates, after He has been known as 'Pardoner', 'Forgiver', 'Generous', that He becomes an enemy because of it to the one who removes this name from Him to every description of every harsh and every vile person; and thereby the one who said deserves, for this is the greatest of sins where he [changed] the attributes of the Lord. Then, by what the prophets were tested, they would be declared disbelievers in those states; and whoever declares a prophet a disbeliever at any time is a disbeliever without doubt. Then, by this, the Lord is described with injustice because it nullifies good deeds by a lapse, while justice is what rewards good and evil in what He, the Mighty and Majestic, has shown of His generosity; then [by] ignorance regarding not knowing who is fit for the message and performs the trust. Then, by that from which no one is [free], there is in what was mentioned obligation beyond capacity; then fear and hope are cut off from him and the matter ends in security and despair, and He has testified upon both as misguidance and disbelief. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two theological positions on minor sins: that of the Mu'tazila, who affirm forgiveness only for minor sins and exclude the act of wisdom (i.e., they require that God must forgive minor sins), and that of the Khawarij, who remove the name of faith from anyone committing a minor sin. The author argues that neither view is tenable. He then turns to a third extreme view—that minor sins are equivalent to kufr or shirk or warrant eternal punishment—and declares it an abandoned (mahuur) statement. The reason is that it contradicts God's names 'Pardoner', 'Forgiver', 'Merciful', implying that God cannot forgive without repentance. This would make God an enemy of those who remove this attribute from Him, which is the greatest sin (changing divine attributes). Furthermore, it would lead to declaring prophets disbelievers due to their minor lapses, which is itself kufr. It also accuses God of injustice by nullifying good deeds due to one lapse, whereas God's justice is balanced with generosity. It implies ignorance of who is fit for prophethood, leads to obligation beyond capacity (since no one is free from minor lapses), and cuts off fear and hope (which is condemned in the Qur'an as misguidance and disbelief). The argument proceeds step by step, each consequence building on the previous to show that the extreme view is rationally and scripturally untenable.

Key Terms
الصغائر
المغفرة
المعتزلة
الخوارج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Maturidi teaches that minor sins are always punished or never forgiven.
Correction: Maturidi does not deny forgiveness; he argues against compulsory forgiveness and against declaring a person a disbeliever for minor sins, preserving God's wisdom and mercy.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the Mu'tazila position on minor sins remove from God?
A) His attribute of justice
B) The act of wisdom
C) His mercy
D) His power

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that requiring forgiveness for minor sins or excommunicating for them both lead to attributing injustice to God, thus they are rejected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own minor sins, do you secretly assume God will always forgive them without fail, or do you fear the possibility of punishment? How does this passage's conditional argument force you to reconsider your assumption, and what does it mean for your daily reliance on God's mercy?
?
Have you ever caught yourself mentally downgrading someone's faith because of a sin they committed? In light of the Khawarij position refuted here, how does attributing injustice to God lie beneath that judgment, and what concrete step will you take to avoid this in your heart tomorrow?
?
The passage says that attributing injustice to God is worse than the sins themselves. Do you ever become angry at God's decree when you face difficulty or feel your sins are 'unfairly' punished? How can the truth that God's wisdom underpins even the possibility of punishment reshape your response to trials and your repentance?

Consequences of Equating Minor Sins with Kufr
Maturidi lists six consequences of equating minor sins with kufr/shirk/eternal punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi considers the view that minor sins are equivalent to kufr or shirk an 'abandoned statement' by examining its numerous theological consequences.

Core Idea
Equating minor sins with kufr contradicts God's names and leads to absurdities.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi continues his refutation of extreme views on minor sins. He now addresses the position that minor sins are tantamount to kufr or shirk or warrant eternal punishment. He declares this view 'abandoned' and then lists several devastating consequences it entails, from nullifying divine mercy to declaring prophets disbelievers and cutting off fear and hope.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that it begins with 'Then, the statement...' and proceeds through several consequences linked by 'ثم' (then).
The structure is enumerative, listing consequences.
2
Identify the first consequence
The first consequence is that this view nullifies the meaning of God's names 'Pardoner', 'Forgiver', 'Merciful', because it implies God cannot forgive any sin without repentance.
This is a direct attack on God's attributes.
3
Trace the remaining consequences
The passage then states that this view leads to declaring prophets disbelievers due to their minor lapses, describes God with injustice by nullifying good deeds with a single lapse, implies ignorance about who is fit for prophethood, imposes obligation beyond capacity, and cuts off fear and hope which are condemned in the Quran.
Each consequence is a separate argument against the extreme view.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how Maturidi uses these consequences to show that the extreme view is not only wrong but leads to multiple theological contradictions and even kufr.
The final statement 'There is no power except with Allah' signals the end.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Different Positions on Major Sinners
Maturidi enumerates the differing Muslim positions on major sin perpetrators.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the range of Muslim scholarly positions on the status of those who commit major sins, as enumerated by Imam Maturidi.

Core Idea
Islamic scholars held at least five distinct views on major sinners, from declaring them disbelievers to retaining their faith.

Imam Maturidi begins by justifying the importance of discussing major sins, then presents a comprehensive list of the positions held by different groups within the Muslim community. This classification is essential for understanding later theological debates on faith, sin, and divine punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the justification
Notice how Maturidi explains why the discussion of major sins is important: even they are subject to forgiveness, so minor sins are even more so, and the disagreement has significant impact on the community.
This sets the stage for the enumeration.
2
Identify the six positions
As you read the enumeration, list each position Maturidi mentions. Pay attention to the categories: kāfir, mushrik, neither believer nor kāfir, munāfiq, believer-fāsiq, and the suspensive view on the threat.
The last position suspends judgment on whether the threat applies to the mustaḥill or others.
3
Reflect on the range
Consider why such a wide range of views exists. The passage suggests that different considerations (desire, heedlessness, hope of forgiveness) lead to different conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر ما قيل في الكبائر، فإنها إذ صارت بحيث احتمال العفو فما دونها أولى؛ وبما للقول به فيها على الاختلاف أثر بيّن في الأمة، فصرف الكلام إليه أحق. وبالله التوفيق. مسألة اختلاف المسلمين في مرتكبي الكبائر ثم اختلفت الأمة في مرتكبي الكبائر من المسلمين دفعته إليه الغلبة من شهوة أو غفلة أو شدة الغضب والحمية أو رجاء العفو والتوبة من غير استحلال منه ولا استخفاف بمن أمر ونهي. فمنهم من جعله كافرًا، ومنهم من جعله مشركاً، ومنهم من جعله غير مؤمن ولا كافر، ومنهم من يجعله منافقاً، ومنهم من جعله مؤمنًا على ما كان عاصيا بما فعل، فاسقاً به من غير أن يطلق له اسم الفسق والفجور إلا مع من يعلم ما به سُمّي بذلك، ويرى أن يكون الله تعذيبه بقدر ذنبه والعفو عنه بما علم منه من الصدق له في العبودة وغيره من الحسنات. ومنهم من وقف في الوعيد أنه أريد به المستحل أو غيره، ورآه واجبًا. فتفريق من ذكرت بين الصغائر والكبائر فيما يثبت في الصغائر من إمكان العفو أو إبقاء اسم الإيمان أوجب صرف الوعيد إلى الكبائر؛ وما يثبت من ذكر جزاء الكفر والشرك ونحوه يوجب تحقيق اسم الشرك على قول قوم، والكفر على قول. وأيّد ذلك قوله: إنه لا ييأس من الله إلا القوم الكافرون [يوسف، ٨٧/١٢]، وقال: ومن يقنط من رحمة ربه إلا الضالون [الحجر، ٥٦/١٥]. ما كان صاحب الكبيرة حاكما بغير الذي أنزل الله، مع وتاركا الحكم به، وقد قال الله تعالى: ومن لم يحكم بما أنزل الله فأولئك هم الكافرون [المائدة، ٤٤/٥]. وبعد، فإنه قد سُمّي بالأسماء التي سمى الله بها الكفرة من الفسق والفجور والظلم لزمه أيضاً اسم الكفر. مع ما قسم الله البشر الذين جرى عليهم القلم فيما عليه أمرهم في الدنيا والآخرة، فقال: هو الذي خلقكم فمنكم كافر ومنكم مؤمن [التغابن، ٢/٦٤]،
Then we mention what has been said regarding major sins. For when they become such that the probability of forgiveness applies to them, then what is lesser than them is more fitting; and because the statement regarding them, despite the disagreement, has a clear impact in the community, directing speech to it is more appropriate. And with Allah is success. Question: The disagreement of Muslims regarding the perpetrators of major sins. Then the community disagreed regarding the perpetrators of major sins among Muslims—those whom desire, heedlessness, intense anger and zeal, or hope of forgiveness and repentance, drove to it, without considering it lawful or belittling the one who commands and forbids. Among them are those who declare him a disbeliever; among them are those who declare him a polytheist; among them are those who declare him neither a believer nor a disbeliever; among them are those who declare him a hypocrite; among them are those who declare him a believer despite being disobedient due to what he did, a sinner thereby, without applying to him the name 'sinner' or 'immoral' except with one who knows that by which he is so named, and he holds that Allah will punish him according to his sin and pardon him because of what He knows of his sincerity in servitude and other good deeds. And among them are those who stopped regarding the threat, that it was intended for the one who considers it lawful or others, and considered it obligatory. So the distinction of those I mentioned between minor and major sins, regarding what is established for minor sins of the possibility of forgiveness or retaining the name of faith, necessitates directing the threat to major sins; and what is established of the mention of the recompense of kufr and shirk and the like necessitates the realization of the name 'shirk' according to some people, and 'kufr' according to others. And that is supported by His saying: 'Indeed, no one despairs of the mercy of Allah except the disbelieving people' [Yusuf 12:87], and He said: 'And who despairs of the mercy of his Lord except those who are astray?' [Hijr 15:56]. It is not the case that the committer of a major sin is one who judges by other than what Allah has revealed, while abandoning judgment by it; and Allah has said: 'And whoever does not judge by what Allah has revealed – then it is those who are the disbelievers' [Ma'ida 5:44]. Moreover, since he has been named with the names by which Allah named the disbelievers, from sinfulness and immorality and injustice, the name of kufr also applies to him. Along with what Allah has divided humanity – those upon whom the pen has passed regarding their affair in this world and the next – so He said: 'He is the one who created you, and among you is a disbeliever and among you is a believer' [Taghabun 64:2],
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the different Muslim positions on the status of those who commit major sins (kabā'ir). The author begins by justifying the discussion: since even major sins are subject to the possibility of forgiveness, minor sins are even more so, and the disagreement among Muslims on this issue is significant and impactful. He then enumerates the positions: some say the perpetrator is a disbeliever (kāfir), some say a polytheist (mushrik), some say neither believer nor disbeliever (i.e., an intermediate position), some say a hypocrite (munāfiq), and some say he remains a believer despite his disobedience, being a sinner (fāsiq) but without applying the label 'sinner' or 'immoral' except in a qualified sense, and they hold that God will punish according to the sin but may forgive due to sincerity and good deeds. Another group suspends judgment on the threatened punishment, considering it either conditional on considering the sin lawful or otherwise, and they view the punishment as obligatory (i.e., the threat is real). The author then notes that the distinction between minor and major sins, such that minor sins allow forgiveness and retention of faith, necessitates that the warnings in scripture apply to major sins. Conversely, applying terms like kufr and shirk to major sins leads some to label the perpetrator as a mushrik or kāfir. He supports the latter view with Quranic verses condemning despair of God's mercy and judging by other than what Allah revealed. He argues that since God applies terms like fisk (sinfulness), fujūr (immorality), and zulm (injustice) to certain actions, the perpetrator of a major sin also incurs the name kufr. The passage ends by citing the division of humanity into believer and disbeliever as a divine decree, implying that such labels are within God's sovereignty.

مرتكبو الكبائر
الكفر
الشرك
الفسق
النفاق
الوعيد
الاستحلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Maturidi takes a single position on major sins.
Correction: Rather, he objectively enumerates multiple views from different Muslim groups.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, which of the following is NOT one of the positions held on perpetrators of major sins?
A) He is a disbeliever
B) He is a polytheist
C) He is a hypocrite
D) He is a believer without any qualification

Key Takeaway
Muslim scholars held at least six distinct positions on the status of major sinners, reflecting deep theological diversity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own major sins, which of the positions listed in this passage do you find yourself instinctively leaning towards? Does that inclination make you more hopeful or more fearful of Allah's judgment?
?
The passage mentions that some sin due to 'hope of forgiveness and repentance.' Is your own hope of Allah's mercy a motivation to repent, or does it ever lead you to take sin lightly? How can you ensure it remains a sincere driver for change?
?
One group suspends judgment on the divine threat. Are there sins in your life whose consequences you are uncertain about? How does that uncertainty affect your urgency to repent and reform?

Arguments and Implications of the Positions on Major Sins
Maturidi presents arguments linking major sins to disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments that support linking major sins to disbelief and the reasoning behind the different positions.

Core Idea
The distinction between minor and major sins, and the application of scriptural threats, lead some scholars to equate major sins with disbelief.

After listing the positions, Maturidi delves into the reasoning that supports them, especially the view that major sins constitute disbelief. He draws on Quranic verses and logical arguments to show how the labels used for disbelievers can apply to major sinners.

Guided Path
1
Trace the argument from minor vs. major
Notice how Maturidi argues that if minor sins allow forgiveness and retention of faith, then the threats must apply to major sins. This sets up the debate.
2
Examine the Quranic proofs
Read the three Quranic verses Maturidi cites (Yusuf 12:87, Hijr 15:56, Ma'ida 5:44). Consider how each one is used to support the view that major sins can lead to kufr.
The verse on judging by other than Allah's revelation is particularly linked to major sins.
3
Understand the naming argument
Maturidi argues that since major sinners are called by the same names as disbelievers (fāsiq, ẓālim, etc.), the name kāfir also applies. This is a key logical move.
4
Reflect on the final verse
The citation of Taghabun 64:2 reinforces that belief and disbelief are divinely decreed categories, but the passage ends without a final conclusion, leaving room for interpretation.

The Five Views on Major Sinners in Islamic Theology
Maturidi enumerates five views on major sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five different positions held by early Muslim scholars regarding the status of a person who commits a major sin without considering it lawful.

Core Idea
Major sins divide Muslim theologians into five distinct camps.

Imam Maturidi opens a crucial discussion by laying out the existing opinions on the fate of those who commit major sins. This passage is not an argument for any one view but a map of the theological landscape. Understanding these categories is essential before engaging with the proofs that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice that Maturidi first justifies discussing major sins before listing the views.
The condition for the sinner is important: he does not consider the sin lawful.
2
Identify the five opinions
The passage lists five distinct positions: kafir, mushrik, neither believer nor disbeliever, munafiq, and believer who is a fasiq.
The last position has a nuance about not applying the label 'fasiq' universally.
3
Understand the context
Maturidi is not endorsing any view here; he is simply cataloging existing opinions within the Muslim community.
This is important for later discussion.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why knowing these categories is foundational for the debate on faith and works.
This connects to broader theological issues.

Then we mention what has been said about major sins, for since they are such that the possibility of pardon applies, then what is less than them is even more [entitled to pardon]; and because [discussing] the statement about them based on the disagreement has a clear impact on the community, directing speech to it is more appropriate. And with Allah is success.

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the different views held by Muslims regarding the status of someone who commits a major sin (kabira) without considering it lawful or being defiant. Maturidi begins by stating that the discussion of major sins is important because if pardon is possible for them, then lesser sins are even more likely to be pardoned. He then enumerates five positions: 1) they are disbelievers (kafir), 2) they are idolaters (mushrik), 3) neither believer nor disbeliever, 4) hypocrites (munafiq), and 5) believers who are transgressors (fasiq) but without applying the label of fisq universally. The passage sets the stage for the arguments to follow.

Maturidi's Refutation of the Mu'tazila on Naming Major Sinners
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila's prohibition of naming major sinners as kafirs and critiques the misuse of names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the Mu'tazila's prohibition of calling major sinners kafirs is baseless, and why names like iman and kufr are not inherently beneficial or harmful.

Core Idea
Names do not change realities; the Mu'tazila's fear of the name kufr is irrational.

Maturidi challenges the Mu'tazila's claim that major sinners should not be called kafirs. He argues that the threat (al-wa'id) is real regardless of naming. He also criticizes both the Hashwiyya and Mu'tazila for expanding the name of iman to every good deed, thereby diminishing its meaning. This passage delves into the philosophy of naming and the nature of hypocrisy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the full translation. Let Maturidi's argument flow naturally.
Pay attention to the sequence: refutation of Mu'tazila, then critique of both groups, then discussion of names, then nifaq.
2
Identify the core argument against Mu'tazila
Find the part where Maturidi states that the Mu'tazila's fear of the name 'kufr' is based on the greatness of the threat, but names themselves carry no inherent benefit or harm.
The key phrase is 'فلم يحصل لهم بما تحرجوا عن التسمية إلا ما كان فزعهم عن اسمه لعظم الوعيد'.
3
Reflect on the nature of names
Maturidi explains that if a name has no hoped benefit or feared harm, it is merely a permission. Connect this to his broader critique of the Mu'tazila's position.
Think about how this principle applies to other theological debates about labeling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وألزموا الوعيد بما في الرفع الحوق الكذب، والله يجل عن ذلك. وكل الذي ذكرت يلزم المعتزلة في منعهم تسمية الكفر. على أن قولين من أقاويل منتحلي الإسلام حصلا في حق الأسماء على عبث وإبطال ما جبل عليه البشر من جلالة قدر الإيمان في قلوبهم، وما عظم الله دين الإسلام في العقول. فصير أحد فريقي الإسلام اسم الإيمان لكل خير، فقطع فزع تبدل دين الإسلام، وأزال جلالة قدره حيث أشركوا في اسمه كل شيء مما يحتمل أن يكون له اسم الخير. فاشترك في هذا الحشوية والمعتزلة. وانفردت المعتزلة بمنع اسم الكفر عن أصحاب الكبائر، على تحقيق جميع ما في الكفر من العقوبة في ذلك. فلم يحصل لهم بما تحرجوا عن التسمية إلا ما كان فزعهم عن اسمه لعظم الوعيد في ذلك؛ وإلا فالتسمية إذا لم تكن لنفع يرجى أو لضرر يتقى، فكانت من المسمين بها إباحة إن ساءت أو حسنت إذا لم تكن يجب بحسنها حسن أو بقبحها قبح ولا قوة إلا بالله. فدخل تسمية الشرك والكفر فيما مربيانه. ومن حقق له اسم النفاق فلمخالفة ما أعطى بلسانه من الإيمان وتعاهد حدوده وحفظ حدود الله ما ظهر بأفعاله، وبذلك قال الله تعالى: وليعلمن الله الذين آمنوا وليعلمن المنافقين، وقال: ألم أحسب الناس أن يتركوا الآية، أخبر بأن.
And they necessitated the threat because raising [it] would entail lying, and Allah is exalted above that. And all that I have mentioned necessitates [applying to] the Mu'tazila in their prohibition of naming disbelief. However, two sayings from the sayings of the claimants to Islam have occurred concerning names as frivolity and nullification of what human nature is disposed to of the majesty of the rank of faith in their hearts, and what Allah magnified the religion of Islam in minds. So one of the two groups of Islam made the name of faith (iman) applicable to every good thing, and thus cut off the anxiety of change in the religion of Islam, and removed the majesty of its rank by associating in its name every thing that could possibly have the name of good. The Hashwiyya and the Mu'tazila both participated in this. And the Mu'tazila were alone in prohibiting the name of disbelief (kufr) from the perpetrators of major sins (sahib al-kaba'ir), despite the realization of all the punishment of disbelief in that. So they did not obtain by their refraining from naming except their fear of its name due to the greatness of the threat in that. Otherwise, naming, if it is not for a hoped benefit or a feared harm, then it is from the named ones as a permission whether it is bad or good, if its goodness does not necessitate goodness or its ugliness does not necessitate ugliness. And there is no power except with Allah. So the naming of shirk and kufr entered into what... And whoever is verified for himself the name of hypocrisy (nifaq), it is due to contradicting what he gave with his tongue of faith in his observance of its limits and preserving the limits of Allah by what appeared in his actions. And with that Allah the Exalted said: 'And Allah will surely make evident those who believe and surely make evident the hypocrites' (al-Ankabut 29:11), and said: 'Alif, Lam, Meem. Do the people think that they will be left...' (al-Ankabut 29:1-2) informing that.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila's stance on prohibiting the name of kufr from major sinners. The author begins by stating that they (the Mu'tazila and like) necessitated the threat (of punishment) because raising it would imply lying, and Allah is above that. Then he says that everything he mentioned applies to the Mu'tazila in their prohibition of naming disbelief. He then critiques two groups among the claimants to Islam: the Hashwiyya and the Mu'tazila. Both groups, he says, acted frivolously regarding names, nullifying the natural majesty of faith. The one group (Hashwiyya and Mu'tazila together) made the name of iman apply to every good thing, thus removing its special status. The Mu'tazila alone prohibited the name kufr from major sinners, even though the punishment of kufr is realized for them. Their fear of the threat caused them to avoid the name, but names have no inherent benefit or harm; they are merely permissions. The author then discusses nifaq (hypocrisy), noting that one is called a hypocrite for contradicting his verbal faith with his actions, as the Quran says.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الحشوية
الكبائر
النفاق
التسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Maturidi condemns the Hashwiyya and Mu'tazila equally for their handling of names.
Correction: Maturidi distinguishes: both groups share the error of expanding iman, but only the Mu'tazila deny the name kufr to major sinners.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the primary reason the Mu'tazila refrain from calling major sinners kafirs?
A) They believe major sinners are not punished
B) They fear the great threat associated with the name
C) They consider the name kufr to have no effect
D) They follow the Hashwiyya in all matters

Key Takeaway
Names are not the source of benefit or harm; the reality behind the name is what matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there truths about yourself that you avoid naming because you fear the weight they carry? What would it cost you to call your sin by its real name today?
?
When you see major sins in others, do you soften your judgment to avoid discomfort? How does Maturidi's insistence on naming according to reality challenge your own tendency to sugarcoat for fear of harm?
?
If you truly believed that the label itself has no power—only the reality does—how would your relationship with forgiveness and accountability change? Would you be more honest or more reckless?

Maturidi's Critique of Mu'tazila on Naming Major Sinners
Maturidi critiques Mu'tazila on naming major sinners

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the Mu'tazila's inconsistent position on naming major sinners and their flawed approach to theological naming.

Core Idea
Naming is neutral; fear of a word does not change reality.

This passage from Maturidi's Kitab al-Tawhid examines a key controversy between the Mu'tazila and mainstream Sunni theology. The Mu'tazila refused to call major sinners disbelievers, even though they accepted that such sinners deserve eternal punishment. Maturidi argues that this inconsistency stems from a misunderstanding of the role of names in theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the dense argumentation style of Maturidi.
The passage contains several interconnected points; don't worry if it feels dense.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's inconsistency
Find where Maturidi says the Mu'tazila are forced to accept the threat (wa'id) but they prohibit naming major sinners as kafir. Why is this inconsistent?
The inconsistency is that they accept the punishment but avoid the label.
3
Understand the two groups' misuse of names
Maturidi mentions two groups: one broadened 'iman' to every good deed (Hashwiyya and Mu'tazila together), and the Mu'tazila uniquely refused to call major sinners kafir. Compare their errors.
Both groups distorted the proper use of theological names.
4
Reflect on Maturidi's principle of naming
At the end, Maturidi states that naming itself is neutral unless it brings benefit or harm. How does this principle undermine the Mu'tazila position?
Fear of the word 'kufr' does not change its applicability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وألزموا الوعيد بما في الرفع الحوق الكذب ، والله يَجِلَّ عن ذلك. وكل الذي ذكرت يُلزم المعتزلة في منعهم تسمية الكفر. على أن قولين من أقاويل منتحلي الإسلام حصلا في حق الأسماء على عبث وإبطال ما جبل عليه البشر من جلالة قدر الإيمان في قلوبهم، وما عظم الله دين الإسلام في العقول. فصيّر أحد فريقي الإسلام اسم الإيمان لكل خير، فقطع فزع تبدل دين الإسلام، وأزال جلالة قدره حيث أشركوا في اسمه كل شيء مما يُحتمل أن يكون له اسم الخير. فاشترك في هذا الحشوية والمعتزلة . وانفردت المعتزلة بمنع اسم الكفر عن أصحاب الكبائر، على تحقيق جميع ما في الكفر من العقوبة في ذلك. فلم يحصل لهم بما تحرجوا عن التسمية إلا ما كان فزعهم عن اسمه لعظم الوعيد في ذلك؛ وإلا فالتسمية إذا لم تكن لنفع يرجى أو لضرر يتقى، فكانت من المسمين بها إباحة إن ساءت أو حسنت إذا لم تكن يجب بحسنها حسن أو بقبحها قبح ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And they were forced to accept the threat (al-wa'id) because raising [something] involves falsehood, and Allah is exalted above that. And everything I have mentioned obligates the Mu'tazila regarding their prohibition of naming (kufr). However, two sayings from the statements of adherents of Islam occurred regarding names, based on frivolity and nullifying what human nature was created upon of the majesty of the status of faith in their hearts, and what Allah magnified of the religion of Islam in intellects. So one of the two groups of Islam made the name 'iman' apply to every good [deed], thus cutting off the fear of alteration of the religion of Islam and removed the majesty of its status when they associated in its name everything that could possibly be called good. In this, al-Hashwiyya and the Mu'tazila shared. And the Mu'tazila were unique in prohibiting the name 'kufr' from the people of major sins, while acknowledging all the punishment that is in kufr in that. So they did not obtain by their caution about naming except that their fear of its name was due to the greatness of the threat in that. Otherwise, naming, if it is not for a hoped benefit or feared harm, then it becomes from the namers a permission, whether it be bad or good, if good is not required by its goodness or evil by its evil. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the Mu'tazila's position on refusing to call major sinners kafir. Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazila are forced to accept the threat of punishment (al-wa'id) for major sins, but they inconsistently avoid labeling those sinners as disbelievers. He then points out a broader issue: two groups of Muslims, the Hashwiyya and the Mu'tazila, have engaged in frivolous naming practices. The Hashwiyya applied the name 'iman' to every good deed, thereby diminishing the special status of faith. The Mu'tazila, while agreeing with the Hashwiyya on that point, uniquely refused to call major sinners kafir, even though they acknowledge the punishment associated with kufr. Their fear of the name stems from the severity of the threat. However, Maturidi argues that naming in itself, if it brings no benefit or harm, is merely a matter of permission, not a cause of goodness or evil. Thus, their position is logically inconsistent.

Key Terms
الوعيد
الكفر
الإيمان
الحشوية
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila refusal to name major sinners kafir is based on mercy or leniency.
Correction: Maturidi argues it is based on a flawed understanding: they fear the word 'kufr' because of its severe threat, but this fear does not justify avoiding the label if it is true.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what did the Mu'tazila and al-Hashwiyya share?
A) Refusal to call major sinners kafir
B) Making the name 'iman' apply to every good deed
C) Denial of the threat (wa'id)
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the Mu'tazila's refusal to name major sinners kafir is inconsistent because they still affirm the punishment, and that naming itself is neutral—its moral value depends on consequences, not the name itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a fellow Muslim committing a major sin, is your first reaction to mentally distance them from 'iman'? How does Maturidi's critique of the Mu'tazila's fear of the name 'kufr' expose your own tendency to judge by labels rather than reality? What would it mean for you to see them as still within the fold despite their sin?
?
Maturidi argues that naming is neutral—it neither causes good nor evil by itself. Is there a word or label you are afraid to use—for yourself or for others—because of its weight? Where in your life are you avoiding a truthful naming out of fear? Will you commit to calling things what they are, trusting that Allah judges the reality, not the label?
?
You call yourself a believer (mu'min). But if the name 'iman' were applied only to the truly faithful, would you still deserve it? Maturidi warns against diluting the name. Are you hiding behind a title that your actions do not support? What is one concrete step you will take today to make your reality match your name?

The Mu'tazila Argument on Naming Major Sinners
Maturidi presents the Mu'tazila's argument on naming major sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's reasoning for classifying major sinners as neither believers nor disbelievers, and their use of the promise and threat.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue that major sinners cannot be called believers because they incur the threat.

The Mu'tazila were known for their rational theology. One of their distinctive positions was that a person who commits a major sin is in an intermediate state—neither believer nor disbeliever. In this passage, al-Maturidi presents their argument concisely before offering his own response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how the Mu'tazila build their case step by step.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila's three main claims
The passage contains three key claims: (1) believers are only called by good names, (2) the promise is attached to the name 'iman', (3) major sinners incur the threat, so they cannot be believers. Find each claim.
3
Understand the two arguments about the threat
The Mu'tazila present two points regarding the threat (wa'id): its generality and the verse about avoiding major sins. Reflect on why these support their position.
Also note their emphasis on eternal punishment as a deterrent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتجت المعتزلة في الاسم بما سُمّي صاحب الكبيرة بأسماء خبيثة، والإيمان من الأسماء الطيبة لا يسمّى بها . مع ما جاء من الوعد باسم الإيمان، والوعد لا يحتمل الخصوص؛ ثم صاحب الكبيرة قد جاء فيه الوعيد فبطل أن يكون مؤمنا ولم يُسم به كافرًا بما لم يرد به التسمية، فسمّي بالذي أجمع أنه له اسم وهو الفسق والفجور والظلم. ثم لهم في الوعيد أمران عموم أخباره، والثاني قوله: إن تجتنبوا كبائر ما تنهون عنه الآية، بين ما لا يغفر ويغفر. مع ما كان الوعيد بالتخليد أعظم في المنع وأبلغ في الزجر، فهو أحق. على أن الوعيد إذا وجب لزم دخول النار، ولم يذكر فيهم الخروج ولا قوة إلا بالله . قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله : نقول وبالله نستعين : أجمع هؤلاء على اختلافهم على أن الوعيد مما لم يُشرك فيه المؤمنون، بل هو في كل ذنب أخرج صاحبه عن الإيمان وأسقط عنه اسمه. والمرجئة توافقهم على أن كل ذنب يُخرج صاحبه عن الإيمان فالوعيد له لازم. ثم إن المرجئة تخاف على المؤمنين فيما ارتكبوا من المأثم مع قيام الإيمان بالعقوبة، وأولئك لا يخافون عليهم؛ وكان احتجاجهم بعموم الآثار فثبت بالذي ذكرت من قول الجملة أن المرجئة، وهي التي أرجأت الذنوب، أشد استعمالاً لها على العموم من الذين
And the Mu'tazila argued regarding the name, because the committer of major sins is called by evil names, and iman is from the good names, so he is not called by it. Along with what came of the promise (al-wa'd) by the name of iman, and the promise does not admit specificity. Then the committer of major sins has the threat (al-wa'id) applied to him, so it is invalid that he be a believer, and he is not called a disbeliever because the name was not applied that way. So he is called by what is agreed that it is a name for him, which is transgression (fisq), immorality (fujur), and injustice (zulm). Then they have two matters regarding the threat: the generality of its reports, and the second is His saying: 'If you avoid the major sins which you are forbidden...' the verse, distinguishing what is not forgiven and what is forgiven. Moreover, the threat of eternal punishment (takhlid) is greater in prevention and more effective as a deterrent, so it is more appropriate. If the threat is obligatory, then entering the Fire is necessary, and no exit is mentioned for them. And there is no power except with Allah. Al-Faqih Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: We say, and by Allah we seek help: All of these, despite their differences, agreed that the threat (al-wa'id) is something in which the believers are not included; rather it applies to every sin that removes its doer from iman and removes its name from him. And the Murji'a agree with them that every sin that removes its doer from iman, the threat necessarily applies to him. Then the Murji'a fear for the believers regarding the sins they commit while iman remains with punishment, but those (the Mu'tazila and others) do not fear for them. And their argument was based on the generality of the reports (athar). So it is established by what I mentioned from the statement of the group that the Murji'a, who postpone the judgment of sins (arja'at al-dhunub), are more strongly applying them (the reports) in generality than those who...
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's objection and al-Maturidi's response. The Mu'tazila argue that a major sinner is called by evil names, not by the good name 'iman'. They say that since the promise of reward is attached to the name 'iman', it cannot apply to sinners, but since the threat is also mentioned for them, they are not called 'kafir' either. Instead, they are called 'fasiq', 'fajir', and 'zalim'. Regarding the threat, the Mu'tazila have two points: the generality of the threatening reports, and the verse about avoiding major sins which distinguishes between forgivable and unforgivable sins. They also emphasize that the threat of eternal punishment is the strongest deterrent. Then al-Maturidi gives his own view: all groups (except the Murji'a) agree that the threat applies only to sins that remove a person from iman. The Murji'a agree that such sins necessitate the threat, but they differ in that they fear punishment for believers who commit sins while still having iman, whereas the Mu'tazila (and Kharijites) do not fear for them. Al-Maturidi concludes that the Murji'a are more consistent in applying the reports generally.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الوعد
الوعيد
الفسق
الفجور
الظلم
التخليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila call major sinners disbelievers.
Correction: The Mu'tazila do not call them disbelievers; they assign them an intermediate status as fasiq.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, why can a major sinner not be called a believer?
A) Because believers are only called by good names, and iman is a good name
B) Because the promise is attached to disbelievers
C) Because major sinners are called disbelievers
D) Because the threat applies only to believers

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila placed major sinners in an intermediate category, excluding them from both believers and disbelievers based on the threat.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know you have committed major sins. What do you call yourself in your heart—a believer, a fasiq, or something else? Does your label reflect the reality of your relationship with Allah’s promise and threat, or do you hide behind a comfortable title?
?
The Mu'tazila used the threat of eternal punishment to deter sin. When you face temptation, does the reality of the Fire genuinely hold you back, or have you become numb to the warnings? What would change today if you truly believed the threat could be applied to you right now?
?
The verse about avoiding major sins distinguishes between what is forgiven and what is not. Are you actively avoiding major sins, or do you presume forgiveness while continuing in them? What step can you take today to turn away from a major sin you have normalized?

Al-Maturidi's Response: Consensus on the Threat's Application
Al-Maturidi responds with the consensus on the threat's limitation to faith-expelling sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Maturidi's rebuttal to the Mu'tazila by appealing to the consensus that the threat applies only to sins that remove one from iman.

Core Idea
Al-Maturidi shows that all groups agree the threat targets sins that expel one from faith.

After presenting the Mu'tazila argument, al-Maturidi offers his own analysis. He points to a consensus among all sects on a foundational point: the threat of punishment is only for sins that take a person out of iman. He then highlights how the Murji'a, despite agreeing on this, differ in their fear for believers.

Guided Path
1
Read the response passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on al-Maturidi's statement of consensus and the subsequent comparison with the Murji'a.
The passage ends abruptly, but the main point is clear.
2
Identify the consensus claim
Al-Maturidi says all groups agreed that the threat is something in which the believers are not included; rather it applies to every sin that removes its doer from iman. Note this carefully.
3
Compare the Murji'a and other groups
Al-Maturidi notes that the Murji'a agree on the threat applying to faith-expelling sins, but they fear punishment for believers who sin, unlike the Mu'tazila and Kharijites. Why does he consider the Murji'a more consistent?
The Murji'a apply the reports more generally.

?

Naming the Major Sinner: Consensus and Innovation
Maturidi states the ummah's consensus on naming the major sinner and criticizes the Mu'tazila's innovation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ummah's consensus on naming the major sinner and why Maturidi criticizes the Mu'tazila's innovation.

Core Idea
The major sinner's name is not neutral—it is a matter of religious consensus.

Imam Maturidi addresses a crucial theological dispute: how should we label a Muslim who commits a major sin? In this passage, he asserts that the Muslim community, despite their differences, agreed on fundamental categories of religious identity: shirk, kufr, and islam. He then criticizes the Mu'tazila for introducing new labels, fasiq and fajir, as an innovation contradicting the consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice the rhythm and key terms.
Pay attention to the repetition of 'lā qūwata illā billāh'.
2
Identify the consensus
Find where Maturidi states the ummah's agreement. What three names do they agree on for the major sinner?
The three names are from among the religions: shirk, kufr, islam.
3
Understand Maturidi's warning
Why does Maturidi say that abandoning these names nullifies the consensus? What role do the Qur'an and Sunnah play?
He warns against caution that leads to doubt.
4
Recognize the Mu'tazila innovation
What two names did the Mu'tazila invent? How do they differ from the agreed-upon names?
They innovated 'fasiq' and 'fajir' in place of 'kafir' and 'mushrik'.
5
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this matters for theology. How does naming affect the status of a major sinner?
This relates to the boundaries of faith and disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأمة ـ على اختلافهم - اتفقت على أن لصاحب الكبيرة اسما من الأديان من شرك أو كفر أو إسلام. فمن أبطلها توقيًا أن ينطق بالشك أبطل ما أجمعوا على القول به، وشهدوا على مجيء الكتاب به والسنة بما لديه يرتفع الريب عمن يُلقي السمع وهو شهيد أو له قلب . ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم القول بالفاسق والفاجر مطلقا مما يُتوزّع فيه ؛ ومن سماه كافرا أو مشركا أطلقه، ومن سماه مؤمنًا أبي ذلك. وكذلك جحدوا اسم أعداء الله ؛ وأبدعت المعتزلة هذين الاسمين على ذينك الاسمين خلافا لما عليه الأمر. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then the community - despite their differences - agreed that the one guilty of a major sin has a name from among the religions: either shirk, kufr, or islam. Whoever nullifies these names out of caution against uttering doubt has nullified what they all agreed upon, and they testified that the Book and the Sunnah came with it, whereby doubt is removed from one who listens attentively and is a witness or has a heart. And there is no power except with God. Then the saying about the fasiq (immoral) and fajir (sinner) in an absolute sense is something that is disputed: whoever calls him a kafir or mushrik speaks unrestrictedly; whoever calls him a believer refuses that. Likewise, they denied the name 'enemies of God'; and the Mu'tazila innovated these two names (fasiq and fajir) in place of those two names (kafir and mushrik), contrary to what is correct. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal argument about the proper naming of the major sinner (ṣāḥib al-kabīra). Maturidi begins by stating the consensus of the Muslim community (al-umma) despite their differences: they agreed that the major sinner has a name from among the categories of religion, which are shirk (associationism), kufr (disbelief), or islām (submission). He then warns that anyone who abandons these names out of fear of uttering doubt has actually nullified what the community agreed upon, and that the Qur'an and Sunnah have confirmed these names, removing doubt from those who listen carefully. Then he addresses the term 'fāsiq' and 'fājir' used absolutely for the major sinner: some call him kāfir or mushrik freely, while others refuse to call him a believer. Maturidi criticizes the Mu'tazila for inventing the terms 'fāsiq' and 'fājir' as labels for the major sinner, replacing the established names of 'kāfir' and 'mushrik'. He declares that this innovation is contrary to the correct understanding (mā ʿalayhi al-amr). The passage ends with the phrase 'lā qūwata illā billāh' repeated.

Key Terms

شرك
كفر
إسلام
فاسق
فاجر
معتزلة
اسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the Mu'tazila's use of 'fasiq/fajir' is a neutral compromise, but Maturidi condemns it as an innovation contradicting the consensus.
Correction: Maturidi explicitly states that the Mu'tazila innovated these two names (fasiq and fajir) in place of kafir and mushrik, contrary to what is correct.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say the ummah agreed upon regarding the major sinner?
A) That he has no name
B) That he has one of three names: shirk, kufr, or islam
C) That he is definitely a fasiq
D) That his name is unknown

Key Takeaway
The ummah's consensus on the categories of shirk, kufr, and islam for the major sinner is foundational, and the Mu'tazila's innovation of fasiq/fajir is a deviation from that consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of someone who has committed a major sin, which name surfaces in your heart: a name from the consensus (shirk, kufr, islam) or a compromise like 'fasiq'? Is your naming a reflection of submission to the ummah's agreement or a personal invention?
?
Are there areas in your life where you avoid calling something by its true name from the Quran and Sunnah because you fear stirring doubt or conflict? How does Maturidi's warning against nullifying the consensus challenge you to be more honest?
?
The Mu'tazila replaced established names with their own innovation. In what ways do you subtly replace the clear categories of revelation with your own comfortable labels? What would it cost you to return to the consensus?

Sects and Disagreements
beginner
7 min
Maturidi's Refutation of the Khawarij on the Forgiveness of Minor Sins
Maturidi refutes Khawarij's misinterpretation of verse 4:48.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the Khawarij interpretation of Quran 4:48 regarding forgiveness of minor sins.

Core Idea
The Khawarij misunderstood the verse: it distinguishes shirk from other sins, not minor sins from major.

The Khawarij sect held a rigid view that those who commit major sins are eternally punished, and they interpreted Quran 4:48 as implying that even minor sins are not forgiven if they are errors. Imam Maturidi begins his refutation by exposing the flaw in their reasoning: the verse is about shirk, not error. Let's see how he argues that the Khawarij's interpretation is corrupt.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the key terms: تأويل, فاسد, ذنب, مغفرة, توبة.
2
Identify the Khawarij's claim
The Khawarij claimed that the verse implies that even minor sins committed in error are not forgiven. Find where Maturidi states this.
The Khawarij interpreted 'from error' (من الخطأ) as referring to the sin.
3
Understand Maturidi's counterargument
Maturidi says the interpretation is 'fāsid' (corrupt) because the verse is about shirk, not about error. He argues that the verse mentions forgiveness explicitly for shirk, and it distinguishes between two sins (shirk and other sins).
Maturidi emphasizes that the verse does not imply repentance because shirk is not forgiven by anything except repentance.
4
Reflect on the lesson's significance
This refutation shows the importance of reading verses in their full context and not forcing them into one's own theological framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قوله : إن الله لا يغفر أن يشرك به الآية ، تأويل الخوارج فيه «من الخطأ» فاسد، لأنه ليس بذنب فيغفر ، وفي هذا ذكر المغفرة؛ ولا يُحتمل إضمار التوبة لما يغفر بمثله الشرك، والآية في التمييز بين الذنبين. وكذلك لا يحتمل قوله : إن تجتنبوا كبائر ما تنهون عنه الآية ، لما فيه التكفير، وما لا ذنب لا يكفّر ، والخطأ لا يحقق الذنب، والتكفير يكون لشيء يُجزى به. ولا يحتمل ما قالت المعتزلة لأن قولهم يمنع تحقيق الشَّبه . هي تقع من مجتنب الكبائر مغفورة ، وفي هذا إثباتها ، ثم التكفير، وهم يجعلونها مغفورة لا مكفّرة، إذ المغفورة هي التي تستر عليها، وفي بقائها إلى مدة دفعها . والمكفّرة هي التي يأتي من صاحبها فعل حسن يكفّر به نحو قوله: فأولئك يبدل الله سيئاتهم حسنات وقوله : وهل أدلكم على تجارة الآية ، وقوله : إن تبدوا الصدقات إلى ذكر التكفير، وكذلك قوله: توبوا إلى الله توبة نصوحا الآية ، وأصله قوله : إن الحسنات يذهبن السيئات . وبعد، فإن الآية لا تحتمل قول المعتزلة، لما هم يجعلون المصرّ على الذنب صاحب الكبيرة، ومن لا يصر عليه فهو تائب عنه نادم عليه ؛ وفي ذلك أنه يغفر بالتوبة، وكل الذنوب تغفر بها والاثنان جريا بالتفريق من قوله : إن الله لا يغفر أن يشرك به الآية ، وقوله : وإن تجتنبوا كبائر ما تنهون عنه الآية . فهو - والله أعلم - أن الشرك لا يغفر إلا بالتوبة عنه، وغير يجوز أن يغفر بالتفضل أو يُكفّر بغيره من الحسنات، ليصح القول مع تحقيق الفائدة لتمييز القرآن. ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم للآية وجوه تمنع قول المعتزلة والخوارج أحدها أنه قال: وإن تجتنبوا كبائر ما تنهون عنه ، وليس في ذلك بيان حكم من لا يجتنب.
Then His saying: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him' [4:48] — the interpretation of the Khawarij regarding it as 'from error' is corrupt, because it is not a sin that is forgiven, and in this there is mention of forgiveness; and it is not plausible to imply repentance for what shirk is forgiven by the like of it, and the verse distinguishes between the two sins. Likewise, His saying: 'If you avoid the major sins from what you are forbidden' [4:31] is not plausible because it involves expiation, and what is not a sin cannot be expiated; and error does not constitute sin, and expiation is for something that is rewarded. And what the Mu'tazila said is not plausible because their statement prevents achieving the similitude. It [the minor sin] occurs from one who avoids the major sins, [and] it is forgiven, and in this there is its affirmation, then expiation; but they make it forgiven, not expiated, since the forgiven is that which is concealed, and its remaining for a period is its repulsion? And the expiated is that from whose perpetrator comes a good deed that expiates it, such as His saying: 'Those, Allah will replace their evil deeds with good' [25:70], and His saying: 'Shall I guide you to a transaction?' [61:10-11], and His saying: 'If you give charity openly' [2:271] with the mention of expiation, and likewise His saying: 'Repent to Allah with sincere repentance' [66:8], and its origin is His saying: 'Indeed, good deeds remove evil deeds' [11:114]. Afterwards, the verse does not admit the Mu'tazila's view, because they make the one who persists in sin the major sinner, and one who does not persist is repentant and remorseful; and in that it is forgiven by repentance, and all sins are forgiven by it; and the two [verses] proceed by distinguishing from His saying: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him' [4:48], and His saying: 'If you avoid the major sins from what you are forbidden' [4:31]. So it is - and Allah knows best - that shirk is not forgiven except by repentance, and other than it is permissible to be forgiven by grace or expiated by other good deeds, so that the statement is correct with the achievement of benefit for the differentiation of the Qur'an. And there is no power except with God. Then the verse has aspects that prevent the Mu'tazila and Khawarij view; one of them is that He said: 'If you avoid the major sins from what you are forbidden' [4:31], and in that there is no statement of the ruling on one who does not avoid.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation by Maturidi of the interpretations of the Khawarij and the Mu'tazila regarding two key Qur'anic verses on forgiveness. He begins with the verse 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him' (al-Nisa' 4:48). The Khawarij interpreted this verse as implying that minor sins committed in error are not forgiven, but Maturidi calls this interpretation corrupt because the verse mentions forgiveness explicitly for shirk, not for error; it distinguishes between shirk and other sins. Then he addresses the verse 'If you avoid the major sins from what you are forbidden' (al-Nisa' 4:31). This verse mentions expiation (takfir) for sins avoided, so it cannot apply to what is not a sin (like error). The Mu'tazila's claim is also implausible because it prevents achieving the proper analogy. Then Maturidi explains the distinction: a minor sin committed by one who avoids major sins is forgiven (maghfūrah), not expiated (mukaffarah). He defines the forgiven as that which is concealed, and the expiated as that which is removed by a good deed, citing several Qur'anic proofs: 'Those, Allah will replace their evil deeds with good' (al-Furqan 25:70), 'Shall I guide you to a transaction?' (al-Saff 61:10-11), 'If you give charity openly' (al-Baqara 2:271), 'Repent to Allah with sincere repentance' (al-Tahrim 66:8), and 'Indeed, good deeds remove evil deeds' (Hud 11:114). He then rejects the Mu'tazila's view that persistence on a sin makes it a major sin and that immediate repentance is required; rather, forgiveness comes through repentance, and all sins can be forgiven that way. The two verses are distinguished: shirk is only forgiven by repentance, while other sins may be forgiven by grace or expiated by good deeds. Finally, he notes that the verse about avoiding major sins has several aspects that block the Mu'tazila and Khawarij positions, and he gives the first one: the verse does not state the ruling for one who does not avoid major sins, leaving room for other possibilities.

فاسد
مغفرة

خطأ
شرك
كبيرة
صغيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Khawarij believed that verse 4:48 implies that even minor sins committed in error are not forgiven.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the verse is specifically about shirk and distinguishes shirk from other sins; it does not address minor sins at all.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is the Khawarij's interpretation of verse 4:48 corrupt?
A) Because the verse does not mention error at all, it mentions shirk
B) Because the Khawarij misquoted the verse
C) Because the verse says Allah forgives all sins except shirk
D) Because the Khawarij are known to be wrong

Key Takeaway
The Khawarij misinterpreted Quran 4:48 by reading a general denial of forgiveness into a verse that specifically addresses shirk.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been too quick to judge someone's faith based on a single verse? How can you guard against misinterpreting Quranic verses like the Khawarij did?
?
Do you sometimes feel that a sin you committed is too great for Allah to forgive? How does Maturidi's clarification of this verse give you hope for Allah's mercy?
?
The Khawarij's harshness led them to exclude others from the faith. In what subtle ways might you be imitating their rigidity in your own judgments of others?
